Reward for Neglect? Early vs. Later Mu'tazilites
First branch: Disagreement on reward for neglect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early and later Mu'tazilite positions on whether reward and punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly and the obligatory.

Core Idea
Neglect is not a positive act, but later Mu'tazilites see it as blameworthy based on Quranic verses.

The Mu'tazilites debated whether neglecting what is ugly or obligatory merits reward and punishment. The early ones argued that non-existence cannot be a cause, while the later ones cited Quranic blame for neglect. This lesson explores their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms الإخلال بالقبيح and الإخلال بالواجب.
The passage contrasts المتقدمون (early) and المتأخرون (later).
2
Understand the early argument
The early Mu'tazilites said no to reward for neglect because non-existence is not a valid cause, and because one neglects countless ugly acts every moment, making it impractical.
This is a rational argument based on causality.
3
Understand the later argument
The later ones affirmed it, citing Quranic verses that blame for disbelief and neglecting prayer and charity.
The evidence is scriptural, not purely rational.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal shift
Notice how the later Mu'tazilites expanded the scope of moral responsibility to include omissions.
This reflects a broader view of moral agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة - من فروع المعتزلة اختلافهم في أن الثواب والعقاب هل يستحقان على الإخلال بالقبيح والإخلال بالواجب؟ فقال المتقدمون: لا إذ العدم لا يصلح علة، وإذ في كل لحظة إخلال بما لا يحصى من القبائح. وقال المتأخرون به لقوله تعالى: {إنه كان لا يؤمن بالله العظيم} {وقالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين} ومنها أنه يجب اقتران الثواب بالتعظيم والعقاب بالإهانة، ودوامهما خلوصهما عن الشوب للعلم الضروري باستحقاق التعظيم والإهانة، ولأن التفضل بالمنافع حسن ابتداء، فإلزام المشاق لأجلها عبث، بخلاف التعظيم، فإنه يحسن من غير استحقاق. ولأن الدوام لطف فيجب، والخلوص أدخل في الترغيب والترهيب. ومنها اختلافهم في وقت الاستحقاق، فقيل: وقت الطاعة والمعصية، وقيل: في الآخرة، وقيل: حالة الاخترام، وقيل: وقت الفعل بشرط الموافاة، وهي أن لا يحيط إلى الموت. في فروع للمعتزلة على استحقاق الثواب والعقاب. منها أنهم بعد الاتفاق على أنه يستحق الثواب والمدح بفعل الواجب
He said: Conclusion – from the branches of the Mu'tazila is their disagreement on whether reward and punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly and neglecting the obligatory. The early ones said: no, because non-existence is not fit to be a cause, and because at every moment there is neglect of countless ugly acts. The later ones affirmed it, based on His saying: 'Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great' and 'And they said: We were not among those who prayed, nor did we feed the poor.' And among them is that it is necessary that reward be coupled with honor and punishment with humiliation, and their permanence and their purity from admixture, due to necessary knowledge of the deserving of honor and humiliation, and because bestowal of benefits is good initially, so imposing hardships for them is vain, unlike honoring, for it is good without deserving. And because permanence is a grace and thus obligatory, and purity is more effective for encouragement and deterrence. And among them is their disagreement on the time of entitlement: it is said: at the time of obedience and disobedience; and it is said: in the Hereafter; and it is said: at the moment of death (al-ikhitram); and it is said: at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwafaat, which is that one does not change until death. In branches of the Mu'tazila on entitlement to reward and punishment. Among them is that after agreeing that one deserves reward and praise for performing the obligatory act
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion (khatimah) that enumerates several Mu'tazilite branches concerning reward and punishment. First, there is disagreement on whether reward/punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly (qabih) and neglecting the obligatory (wajib). The early Mu'tazilites said no, because non-existence cannot be a cause, and one neglects countless ugly things every moment. The later Mu'tazilites affirmed it, citing Quranic verses that blame for disbelief and for neglecting prayer and charity. Second, it is necessary that reward be coupled with honor (ta'zim) and punishment with humiliation (ihanah), and that both be permanent and pure from admixture, based on necessary knowledge of desert and because imposing hardships for benefits without honor is vain. Third, there is disagreement on the time of entitlement: some say at the time of the act, others in the Hereafter, others at the moment of death (ikhtiram), and others at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwafaat (remaining in that state until death). Finally, it begins another branch: after agreeing that reward and praise are deserved for performing the obligatory act, but the text breaks off. Each branch highlights the rationalist method of the Mu'tazila in deriving rules of divine recompense.

Key Terms
الإخلال بالقبيح
الإخلال بالواجب
المتقدمون
المتأخرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward and punishment are only for positive actions, not for omissions.
Correction: Later Mu'tazilites argue that neglect of obligatory or ugly acts can also warrant reward or punishment, based on Quranic verses that blame for failures to act.

Checkpoint
According to the early Mu'tazilites, why is reward not deserved for neglecting the ugly?
A) Because neglect is not a real act
B) Because non-existence cannot be a cause, and neglect is infinite
C) Because the Quran does not mention it
D) Because punishment is only for deliberate sins

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on whether negligence deserves recompense, with early ones rejecting it on rational grounds and later ones affirming it using Quranic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one obligatory act you have neglected recently. If neglect itself is punishable, as the later Mu'tazilites argued, how does that change your view of that omission? What will you do about it today?
?
The early Mu'tazilites reasoned that non-existence cannot be a cause for punishment. Does that reasoning ever make you feel less responsible for what you fail to do? Should it? Examine your heart: do you rely on such rationalizations to avoid accountability?
?
The Quranic verses cited condemn the people of Hell for not praying and not feeding the poor. If you were asked right now, 'Were you among those who pray and feed the needy?' could you answer without hesitation? If not, what concrete step will you take this week to ensure you are not among those who neglect?

Coupling Reward with Honor and Punishment with Humiliation
Second branch: Necessity of coupling with honor and humiliation, permanence, purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that reward and punishment must be accompanied by honor and humiliation, and must be permanent and pure.

Core Idea
Divine reward honors, divine punishment humiliates; both must be eternal and unmixed.

The Mu'tazilites held that reward necessarily implies honor, and punishment humiliation, based on necessary knowledge. They also argued that these must be permanent and pure to be effective for encouragement and deterrence. This lesson breaks down their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on coupling
Read the Arabic text focusing on اقتران الثواب بالتعظيم والعقاب بالإهانة.
The word اقتران means coupling or conjunction.
2
Understand the basis in necessary knowledge
They claim we know by necessity that deserving honor or humiliation follows from obedience or disobedience.
This is an appeal to innate moral intuition.
3
Analyze the analogy with benefits
They contrast bestowing benefits (which is good initially) with imposing hardships for those benefits (which would be vain without honor).
This shows that reward without honor would be pointless.
4
Grasp the arguments for permanence and purity
Permanence is a grace (لطف) and thus obligatory; purity from admixture enhances encouragement and deterrence.
This is a teleological argument about the purpose of reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة - من فروع المعتزلة اختلافهم في أن الثواب والعقاب هل يستحقان على الإخلال بالقبيح والإخلال بالواجب؟ فقال المتقدمون: لا إذ العدم لا يصلح علة، وإذ في كل لحظة إخلال بما لا يحصى من القبائح. وقال المتأخرون به لقوله تعالى: {إنه كان لا يؤمن بالله العظيم} {وقالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين} ومنها أنه يجب اقتران الثواب بالتعظيم والعقاب بالإهانة، ودوامهما خلوصهما عن الشوب للعلم الضروري باستحقاق التعظيم والإهانة، ولأن التفضل بالمنافع حسن ابتداء، فإلزام المشاق لأجلها عبث، بخلاف التعظيم، فإنه يحسن من غير استحقاق. ولأن الدوام لطف فيجب، والخلوص أدخل في الترغيب والترهيب. ومنها اختلافهم في وقت الاستحقاق، فقيل: وقت الطاعة والمعصية، وقيل: في الآخرة، وقيل: حالة الاخترام، وقيل: وقت الفعل بشرط الموافاة، وهي أن لا يحيط إلى الموت. في فروع للمعتزلة على استحقاق الثواب والعقاب. منها أنهم بعد الاتفاق على أنه يستحق الثواب والمدح بفعل الواجب
He said: Conclusion – from the branches of the Mu'tazila is their disagreement on whether reward and punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly and neglecting the obligatory. The early ones said: no, because non-existence is not fit to be a cause, and because at every moment there is neglect of countless ugly acts. The later ones affirmed it, based on His saying: 'Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great' and 'And they said: We were not among those who prayed, nor did we feed the poor.' And among them is that it is necessary that reward be coupled with honor and punishment with humiliation, and their permanence and their purity from admixture, due to necessary knowledge of the deserving of honor and humiliation, and because bestowal of benefits is good initially, so imposing hardships for them is vain, unlike honoring, for it is good without deserving. And because permanence is a grace and thus obligatory, and purity is more effective for encouragement and deterrence. And among them is their disagreement on the time of entitlement: it is said: at the time of obedience and disobedience; and it is said: in the Hereafter; and it is said: at the moment of death (al-ikhitram); and it is said: at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwafaat, which is that one does not change until death. In branches of the Mu'tazila on entitlement to reward and punishment. Among them is that after agreeing that one deserves reward and praise for performing the obligatory act
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion (khatimah) that enumerates several Mu'tazilite branches concerning reward and punishment. First, there is disagreement on whether reward/punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly (qabih) and neglecting the obligatory (wajib). According to the Mu'tazilites, why must reward be coupled with honor?
A) Because honor is a benefit that makes reward valuable

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment must be accompanied by honor and humiliation, and be eternal and pure, based on rational necessity and the purpose of motivating good and deterring evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah, do you truly feel honored, or do you sometimes see obedience as burdensome? How does your expectation of eternal honor affect your worship today?
?
Consider a sin you committed and 'got away with.' Do you feel humiliated before Allah? If not, does that indicate a lack of understanding of His punishment as described in this lesson?
?
If reward and punishment are to be pure and permanent, how does that change your view of worldly pleasures and pains? Are you living today in light of that eternal reality?

Timing of Entitlement to Reward and Punishment
Third branch: Disagreement on timing of entitlement; start of next branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite disagreements on the time at which reward and punishment become deserved, including the concept of al-muwafaat.

Core Idea
When exactly does one deserve reward or punishment? The Mu'tazilites had four opinions.

The Mu'tazilites differed on the precise moment when one becomes entitled to reward or punishment: at the time of the act, in the Hereafter, at death, or at the act with the condition of al-muwafaat (remaining in that state until death). This lesson examines these views and introduces the incomplete next branch.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on timing
Read the Arabic text on اختلافهم في وقت الاستحقاق.
Notice the four different opinions listed.
2
Identify the four views
The views are: (1) at time of act, (2) in the Hereafter, (3) at death (ikhtiram), (4) at act with condition of al-muwafaat.
Al-muwafaat means that one does not change until death (e.g., does not repent or apostatize).
3
Understand the incomplete branch
The passage then begins another branch: after agreeing on reward for performing obligatory acts, but it cuts off. This sets up a new discussion.
This is a separate topic about reward for fulfilling obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة - من فروع المعتزلة اختلافهم في أن الثواب والعقاب هل يستحقان على الإخلال بالقبيح والإخلال بالواجب؟ فقال المتقدمون: لا إذ العدم لا يصلح علة، وإذ في كل لحظة إخلال بما لا يحصى من القبائح. وقال المتأخرون به لقوله تعالى: {إنه كان لا يؤمن بالله العظيم} {وقالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين} ومنها أنه يجب اقتران الثواب بالتعظيم والعقاب بالإهانة، ودوامهما خلوصهما عن الشوب للعلم الضروري باستحقاق التعظيم والإهانة، ولأن التفضل بالمنافع حسن ابتداء، فإلزام المشاق لأجلها عبث، بخلاف التعظيم، فإنه يحسن من غير استحقاق. ولأن الدوام لطف فيجب، والخلوص أدخل في الترغيب والترهيب. ومنها اختلافهم في وقت الاستحقاق، فقيل: وقت الطاعة والمعصية، وقيل: في الآخرة، وقيل: حالة الاخترام، وقيل: وقت الفعل بشرط الموافاة، وهي أن لا يحيط إلى الموت. في فروع للمعتزلة على استحقاق الثواب والعقاب. منها أنهم بعد الاتفاق على أنه يستحق الثواب والمدح بفعل الواجب
He said: Conclusion – from the branches of the Mu'tazila is their disagreement on whether reward and punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly and neglecting the obligatory. The early ones said: no, because non-existence is not fit to be a cause, and because at every moment there is neglect of countless ugly acts. The later ones affirmed it, based on His saying: 'Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great' and 'And they said: We were not among those who prayed, nor did we feed the poor.' And among them is that it is necessary that reward be coupled with honor and punishment with humiliation, and their permanence and their purity from admixture, due to necessary knowledge of the deserving of honor and humiliation, and because bestowal of benefits is good initially, so imposing hardships for them is vain, unlike honoring, for it is good without deserving. And because permanence is a grace and thus obligatory, and purity is more effective for encouragement and deterrence. And among them is their disagreement on the time of entitlement: it is said: at the time of obedience and disobedience; and it is said: in the Hereafter; and it is said: at the moment of death (al-ikhitram); and it is said: at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwafaat, which is that one does not change until death. In branches of the Mu'tazila on entitlement to reward and punishment. Among them is that after agreeing that one deserves reward and praise for performing the obligatory act
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion (khatimah) that enumerates several Mu'tazilite branches concerning reward and punishment. First, there is disagreement on whether reward/punishment are deserved for neglecting the ugly (qabih) and neglecting the obligatory (wajib). The early Mu'tazilites said no, because non-existence cannot be a cause, and one neglects countless ugly things every moment. The later Mu'tazilites affirmed it, citing Quranic verses that blame for disbelief and for neglecting prayer and charity. Second, it is necessary that reward be coupled with honor (ta'zim) and punishment with humiliation (ihanah), and that both be permanent and pure from admixture, based on necessary knowledge of desert and because imposing hardships for benefits without honor is vain. Third, there is disagreement on the time of entitlement: some say at the time of the act, others in the Hereafter, others at the moment of death (ikhtiram), and others at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwafaat (remaining in that state until death). Finally, it begins another branch: after agreeing that reward and praise are deserved for performing the obligatory act, but the text breaks off. Each branch highlights the rationalist method of the Mu'tazila in deriving rules of divine recompense.

وقت الاستحقاق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward and punishment are always determined at the time of the action without any future conditions.
Correction: Some Mu'tazilites conditioned entitlement on remaining steadfast until death (al-muwafaat), showing that the final state matters.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites debated whether reward and punishment are deserved immediately, in the Hereafter, at death, or at the act contingent on steadfastness until death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilites debated whether you deserve reward or punishment at the moment of action, in the Hereafter, at death, or only if you remain steadfast. Which view truly makes you examine the sincerity and consistency of your faith? Are you living as if your next moment could be your last, or as if you have endless time to correct your state?
?
Consider the condition of al-muwafaat: that your final state determines your entitlement. If you were to die right now, would you be secure in that condition? What one change today would align your life with the state you want to be found in at death?
?
The Mu'tazilites offered multiple opinions on timing—this itself shows uncertainty about how divine justice works. Does the lack of a single 'right' answer unsettle you, or free you to take personal responsibility? How does this uncertainty affect your daily effort to obey and avoid sin?

Mu'tazili Disagreement on Neglect as Basis for Reward and Punishment
Presents the Mu'tazili disagreement on whether reward/punishment is deserved for neglecting the ugly and obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili disagreement over whether neglecting the ugly and obligatory itself deserves reward or punishment, and the reasoning of both early and late schools.

Core Idea
Can omission alone deserve reward or punishment? The Mu'tazila disagreed.

This passage from al-Maqasid presents a key intra-Mu'tazili dispute about the foundation of desert for reward and punishment. The early Mu'tazila rejected that mere neglect could be a cause, while later scholars accepted it based on Quranic verses blaming people for omissions. Understanding this distinction clarifies how different schools view the relationship between actions and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read and Note the Two Camps
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two groups: the early Mu'tazila (mutqadimūn) and the later Mu'tazila (muta'akhkhirūn).
The passage is a summary of a disagreement within the Mu'tazila school.
2
Understand the Early Position
Focus on the early Mu'tazila's two reasons against neglect as a basis: non-existence (adam) cannot be a cause for an existent effect, and every moment involves countless neglects making it impractical.
This shows a rationalist approach to causation.
3
Understand the Later Position
Examine the later Mu'tazila's scriptural evidence: Quranic verses that blame for not believing and not praying/feeding, indicating that omission itself can be blameworthy.
They use revealed texts to argue for neglect as a cause.
4
Consider the Theological Implications
Reflect on the question: can non-existence be a cause for divine desert? This touches on broader issues of moral responsibility and divine justice.
This disagreement has implications for theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة - (خاتمة) من فروع المعتزلة اختلافهم في أن الثواب والعقاب هل يستحقان على الإخلال بالقبيح والإخلال بالواجب؟ فقال المتقدمون: لا إذ العدم لا يصلح علة، وإذ في كل لحظة إخلال بما لا يحصى من القبائح. وقال المتأخرون به لقوله تعالى: إنه كان لا يؤمن بالله العظيم قالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين.
He said: Conclusion - (Conclusion) Among the branches of the Mu'tazila is their disagreement over whether reward (thawab) and punishment ('iqaab) are deserved for neglecting (ikhlal) the ugly (qabeeh) and neglecting the obligatory (wajib). The early ones said: No, because non-existence (adam) is not fit to be a cause, and because in every moment one neglects countless ugly acts. The later ones agreed with it due to His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great' and they said, 'We were not of those who prayed, nor did we feed the needy.'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage opens a 'khātima' (conclusion) discussing branches of Mu'tazili thought. The first branch concerns whether reward and punishment are deserved specifically for the act of neglecting (ikhlal) the ugly and the obligatory. The early Mu'tazila (mutqadimūn) answered in the negative, giving two reasons: First, non-existence (adam) cannot serve as a cause (ʿilla) for reward/punishment, since reward/punishment are existent things that require an existent cause. Second, every person neglects innumerable ugly acts at every moment, so if neglect itself were blameworthy, this would lead to impossible consequences. The later Mu'tazila (muta'akhkhirūn) agreed that neglect itself can be a basis for deserving punishment, citing Quranic verses that blame people for not believing and for not praying or feeding the needy. They interpreted these verses as showing that the mere omission of an obligatory act (like faith or prayer) is sufficient for punishment, without requiring a positive commission of a bad act.

Key Terms
إخلال
قبيح
واجب
عدم
متقدمون
متأخرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the disagreement is about whether reward/punishment occur at all, but it is about the basis (illa) for deserving them—whether omission itself suffices.
Correction: Both early and late Mu'tazila agree that reward and punishment are real; they disagree on whether neglect alone, without a positive act, can be the cause for desert.

Checkpoint

A) Because they denied the concept of reward and punishment.
B) Because non-existence cannot be a cause for an existent effect.
C) Because they only accepted punishment for major sins.
D) Because they believed neglect is always unintentional.

Key Takeaway
The early Mu'tazila argued that non-existence cannot be a cause for divine desert, while later Mu'tazila accepted that omission itself can be blameworthy, using Quranic verses as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your daily acts of worship as mere duties to be performed, or do you recognize that neglecting them might be blameworthy in God's sight? How would your prayer life change if you truly believed omission itself could incur His displeasure?
?
You constantly neglect countless potential good deeds each moment. Does that reality make you despair of God's mercy, or does it drive you to focus on fulfilling what He has explicitly obligated? Which response does this lesson's argument encourage in you?
?
If you were to list the obligations you consistently neglect—even 'small' ones—how would you justify that neglect before God? Are you willing to change one such habit today, starting with this very thought?

Four Mu'tazili Opinions on Timing of Reward and Punishment
Enumerates four opinions on timing of deserving

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four different opinions among the Mu'tazila regarding when reward and punishment become deserved.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed on when deserving starts: at act, death, Hereafter, or with persistence.

This lesson presents a concise enumeration of four rival positions within Mu'tazili theology concerning the precise moment at which an act’s recompense takes effect. While the debate may seem technical, it reveals deeper assumptions about divine justice and human responsibility. Read carefully to see how each opinion locates the time of deserving.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall sense of the four opinions.
Notice the concise style; each opinion is introduced by 'qīla' (it was said).
2
Identify the four opinions
List the four opinions in your own words. Pay attention to the key terms: waqt al-ṭā‘a, al-ākhira, ḥālat al-ikhtirām, waqt al-fi‘l bi-sharṭ al-mufāwāt.
The fourth opinion includes a condition; try to understand what 'mufāwāt' means.
3
Reflect on the condition in the fourth opinion
Consider what it means for deserving to be conditional on the person not dying before recompense. How does this differ from the other opinions?
The Arabic phrase 'an lā yuḥīṭa ilā al-mawt' is ambiguous; think about possible interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في وقت الاستحقاق، فقيل: وقت الطاعة والمعصية، وقيل: في الآخرة، وقيل: حالة الاخترام، وقيل: وقت الفعل بشرط الموافاة، وهي أن لا يحيط إلى الموت.
And among them is their disagreement concerning the time of deserving (istihqaq). It was said: at the time of obedience and disobedience. And it was said: in the Hereafter. And it was said: at the state of death (ikhtiram). And it was said: at the time of the act with the condition of persistence (mufawaat), which is that he does not [die?] until death.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This short passage enumerates four distinct opinions among the Mu'tazila regarding the precise time at which reward (thawab) or punishment ('iqaab) becomes deserved (istihqaq) for the morally responsible person (mukallaf). The first opinion holds that it is at the very moment of performing obedience or disobedience. The second opinion postpones it to the Hereafter (al-akhira). The third opinion places it at the moment of death (ikhtiram, meaning being cut off or intercepted by death). The fourth opinion ties it to the time of the act but with the condition of mufawaat, which means that the person persists in not dying until the time of recompense; the exact wording 'an la yuhiṭa ila al-mawt' is ambiguous—possibly meaning 'that he does not die before that' or 'that he is not overtaken by death.' This branch shows the diversity of Mu'tazili thought on when the deserving actively takes effect.

Key Terms

وقت
طاعة
معصية
آخرة
اخترام
موافاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that reward and punishment are always deserved at the moment of the act, without any temporal condition.
Correction: The Mu'tazila debated this issue; some held that deserving occurs at death, others in the Hereafter, and yet others at the act but conditional on not dying before recompense.

Checkpoint

A) The person must persist in obedience until death.
B) The person must not die before the act of recompense.
C) The person must persist in disbelief.
D) The punishment is deferred until the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
The timing of deserving reward and punishment is a debated Mu'tazili issue, with four main positions: at the act, at death, in the Hereafter, or at the act conditioned on not dying before recompense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every single act of obedience or disobedience immediately made you deserving of reward or punishment, what would you change about how you live today? Name one specific action you would stop or start right now because of that belief.
?
Consider the opinion that deserving is postponed until the Hereafter. Does that belief give you false comfort about sins you commit, or does it increase your hope in God's mercy? Be honest: which feeling is stronger in your heart today, and what does that reveal about your faith?
?
The condition of mufawaat requires that you persist in your state until the end. Are you currently living with persistence towards righteousness, or do you waver between good and bad? Commit to one concrete step you will take this week to maintain steadfastness in a specific area of your life.

Detailed Conditions for Reward and the Disagreement on Neglect
Specifies conditions for reward and presents the disagreement on neglect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed conditions for deserving reward and punishment according to the Mu'tazila, and the disagreement over whether the mere neglect of evil merits reward.

Core Idea
Reward depends on intention and action; neglect alone does not suffice according to early Mu'tazila.

This passage lays out the sophisticated Mu'tazili framework for moral desert. The first part specifies the precise intentions required for acts to earn reward or punishment. The second part revisits a key debate: can the mere omission of an evil act be rewarded? The early and later Mu'tazila disagree, with each side citing reason or revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its English translation. Notice the structure: first the agreed-upon conditions, then the disagreement.
2
Identify the conditions for reward
List the three types of actions that deserve reward: obligatory, recommended, and abandoning the ugly. Note the required intention for each.
The intention must align with the type of act.
3
Understand the role of intention
Notice that performing the opposite of the ugly must be done specifically as an abandonment of the ugly, e.g., doing a permissible act to avoid the forbidden. This shows intention matters.
4
Analyze the disagreement on neglect
The early Mu'tazila argue negation cannot cause existential reward; the later Mu'tazila cite Quranic verses blaming omission. Compare their reasoning.
Later ones use nusus.
5
Reflect on the implications
How does this disagreement affect the concept of moral responsibility? Does it make sense to reward someone for not doing evil?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في فروع للمعتزلة على استحقاق الثواب والعقاب. منها أنهم بعد الاتفاق على أنه يستحق الثواب والمدح بفعل الواجب والمندوب، وفعل ضد القبيح، بشرط أن يكون فعل الواجب لوجوبه كالواجب المعين، أو لوجه وجوبه كالواجب المخير، وفعل المندوب لندبيته، أو لوجه ندبيته، وفعل ضد القبيح لكونه تركاً للقبيح بأن يفعل المباح لكونه تركاً للحرام، ويستحق العذاب والذم بفعل القبيح. اختلفوا في أنه هل يستحق المدح والثواب بالإخلال بالقبيح لكونه إخلالاً به، والذم والعقاب على الإخلال بالواجب؟ فقال المتقدمون: لا بل إنما يستحق المدح والثواب بفعل عند الإخلال بالقبيح هو ترك القبيح، والذم والعقاب على فعل عند الإخلال بالواجب هو ترك الواجب، لأن الإخلال عدمي لا يصلح علة للاستحقاق الوجودي، ولأن كل أحد يخل كل لحظة بما لا يتناهى من القبائح. وقال المتأخرون كأبي هاشم وأبي الحسين وعبد الجبار: نعم، للنصوص الصريحة في تعليل العقاب بعدم الإتيان بالواجب، كقوله تعالى: {خذوه فغلوه... إلى قوله: إنه كان لا يؤمن بالله العظيم ولا يحض على طعام المسكين} وكقوله حكاية ما سلككم في سقر قالوا لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين.
Among the branches of the Mu'tazila concerning the deservingness of reward (thawab) and punishment ('iqaab). Among them is that after agreement that one deserves reward and praise (madh) for performing the obligatory (wajib) and the recommended (mandub), and for performing the opposite of the ugly (qabeeh), on condition that the performance of the obligatory is for its obligatoriness, like the determinate obligatory (wajib mu'ayyan), or for the aspect of its obligatoriness, like the optional obligatory (wajib mukhayyar), and the performance of the recommended for its recommendability, or for the aspect of its recommendability, and the performance of the opposite of the ugly for its being an abandonment of the ugly, such that one performs the permissible (mubah) as an abandonment of the forbidden (haram), and one deserves punishment and blame (dhamm) for performing the ugly. They disagreed over whether one deserves praise and reward for neglecting (ikhlal) the ugly because it is a neglect of it, and blame and punishment for neglecting the obligatory. The early ones said: No. Rather, one only deserves praise and reward for an act that is, when neglecting the ugly, the abandonment of the ugly, and blame and punishment for an act that is, when neglecting the obligatory, the abandonment of the obligatory, because neglect is non-existential and not fit to be a cause for existential deservingness, and because everyone neglects countless ugly acts every moment. And the later ones, such as Abu Hashim, Abu al-Husayn, and Abd al-Jabbar, said: Yes, due to explicit texts (nusus) that explain punishment for not performing the obligatory, such as His saying, the Exalted: 'Seize him and shackle him... until His saying: Indeed, he did not believe in Allah, the Most Great, nor did he encourage the feeding of the needy' and as His saying narrating: 'What caused you to enter Saqar? They said: We were not of those who prayed, nor did we feed the needy.'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed criteria for deserving reward and then revisits the earlier disagreement on neglect. It begins by stating the agreed-upon conditions: one deserves reward and praise (thawab wa madh) for performing the obligatory (wajib) and recommended (mandub) acts, and for performing the opposite of the ugly (qabeeh)—i.e., abandoning it. Importantly, the act must be done with the correct intention: the wajib must be performed because it is wajib (or for the aspect of its wajibness in case of a mukhayyar wajib), the mandub for its recommendability, and abandoning the ugly must be done specifically as an abandonment of the ugly (e.g., doing a permissible act with the intention of avoiding the forbidden). Conversely, one deserves punishment and blame for performing the ugly. Then it presents the disagreement: Does one deserve praise for the mere neglect of the ugly, and blame for neglect of the wajib? The early Mu'tazila said no—what deserves praise is the positive act of abandoning the ugly, not the negation itself; similarly, blame attaches to the positive act of abandoning the wajib, not the mere omission. Their reasons: neglect is non-existential and cannot cause existential effects, and everyone neglects countless ugly acts constantly. The later Mu'tazila (Abu Hashim, Abu al-Husayn, Abd al-Jabbar) said yes, citing explicit Quranic verses that blame people for not doing the wajib—e.g., 'he did not believe... nor encourage feeding the needy' and the people in Hell say 'we were not of those who prayed, nor did we feed the needy.' These texts show that the omission itself is grounds for punishment.

Key Terms
واجب
مندوب
قبيح
إخلال
عدم
علة
استحقاق
نصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement is only about punishment for neglecting the wajib.
Correction: It also concerns reward for neglecting the ugly. The early Mu'tazila deny that mere neglect of evil merits reward, while later ones affirm it can be punishable and rewardable.

Checkpoint
What condition does the passage specify for performing the opposite of the ugly (qabeeh) to deserve reward?
A) The act must be obligatory.
B) The act must be performed as an abandonment of the ugly, such as doing the permissible to avoid the forbidden.
C) The act must be done with a sincere heart.
D) The act must be recommended by scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agree on many conditions for desert, but split on whether non-action (neglect) can be a direct basis for reward or punishment, with the later school citing scriptural support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a sin, do you consciously intend to please Allah by abandoning it, or are you merely not doing it? Your answer determines whether you earn reward according to the early Mu'tazila. What is your intention right now?
?
The people of Hell confessed: 'We were not of those who prayed' – punished for omission. Which obligatory acts—prayer, charity, kindness—are you neglecting today, and what is stopping you from starting this moment?
?
The early Mu'tazila argue that everyone neglects countless ugly acts every moment. Are you complacent about the evil you don't commit, or do you actively shun them for Allah's sake? Repentance isn't just leaving sin; it's turning away with resolve.

Four Views on Timing of Desert
Enumeration of four views on timing of desert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main opinions on when reward and punishment are actually deserved (istiḥqāq) as reported by al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree whether desert begins at the act, at death, or in the Hereafter.

Al-Sanusi reports a key theological disagreement among the Mu'tazila and others: at what point does a person actually become deserving of reward or punishment? The answer affects how we understand God's justice and the relationship between actions and their consequences. In this lesson, we will survey the four main positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the views
Read the Arabic passage once to get the list of opinions. Then read the translation.
Notice the terms: Basrians, Baghdadians, ikhtirām, muwāfāh.
2
Identify each group and their position
Match each named group or phrase to the specific timing they propose.
3
Consider the significance
Think about how each position affects the relationship between actions and ultimate consequences.
This disagreement has implications for God's justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في وقت استحقاق الثواب والعقاب. فعند البصرية حالة الطاعة والمعصية ، وعند البغدادية في الآخرة، وقيل في حال الاخترام ، وقيل : وقت الفعل بشرط الموافاة، وهو أن لا تحبط الطاعة والمعصية الى الموت. وليس لأحدهم تمسك يعول عليه سوى ما قيل بأن المدح والذم يثبتان حال الفعل . فكذا الثواب والعقاب لكونهما من موجبات الفعل مثلهما وإنما حسن تأخير تمام الثواب إلى دار الآخرة لمانع ، وهو لزوم الجمع بين المتنافيين . فإن من شرط الثواب الخلوص عن شوب المشاق، ومن لوازم التكليف الشوب بها. وتمسك الآخرون بالنصوص المقتضية لتأخير الأجزية، وبلزوم الجمع بين المتنافيين كما ذكر، ولا خفاء في أن ذلك لا ينافي ثبوت الاستحقاق في دار التكليف. والظاهر أن مراد الأولين ثبوت أصل الاستحقاق، ومراد الآخرين وجوب الأداء ، وقال بعضهم الحق أن التكليف لا يجامع كل الجزاء للزوم المحال بخلاف البعض كتعظيم المؤمن، ونصرته على الأعداء وكالحدود فإنه بجامع التكليف، فلم يجب تأخيره .
And among them is their disagreement concerning the time of deserving reward and punishment. According to the Basrians, [it is] at the moment of obedience and disobedience. According to the Baghdadians, in the Hereafter. And it is said at the time of death. And it is said: at the time of the act with the condition of al-muwāfāh, which is that the obedience and disobedience are not nullified until death. And not one of them has a support to rely on except that it is said that praise and blame are established at the moment of the act. So likewise reward and punishment, because they are among the necessary consequences of the act like them. However, deferring the complete reward to the abode of the Hereafter is good due to an obstacle, which is the necessity of combining mutually exclusive things. For one condition of reward is purity from any admixture of hardships, and one of the requirements of obligation is admixture with them. And the others adhere to the texts requiring deferment of requitals, and to the necessity of combining mutually exclusive things as mentioned, and there is no doubt that this does not negate the establishment of desert in the abode of obligation. The apparent meaning is that the former intend the establishment of the root of desert, and the latter intend the obligation of payment. And some said the truth is that obligation does not coexist with the entire requital due to the impossibility [of combination], unlike some [partial requitals] such as honoring the believer, supporting him against enemies, and the prescribed punishments, for they coexist with obligation, so their deferment is not necessary.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage classifies the different opinions on when reward and punishment are actually deserved (istiḥqāq). The Basrian Mu'tazila hold that desert occurs at the moment of the act itself. The Baghdadian Mu'tazila say it is in the Hereafter. Some say at the moment of death (ikhtirām). Others say at the time of the act but conditioned by al-muwāfāh, meaning the act remains valid until death without being nullified. The author then critiques these views: only the argument about praise and blame being simultaneous with the act has some strength, since reward and punishment are similar consequences. However, deferring full reward to the Hereafter is good because reward must be pure from hardship, whereas obligation inherently involves hardship. The other group insists on textual proofs for deferment. The author clarifies that this does not contradict desert existing in this world: the former group means the root of desert (aṣl al-istiḥqāq), while the latter means the actual payment (wujūb al-adā'). A final opinion is given: obligation cannot coexist with full requital due to logical impossibility, but partial requitals like honoring believers or imposing legal penalties can coexist, so they need not be deferred.

Key Terms
استحقاق
ثواب
عقاب
بصرية
بغدادية
اخترام
موافاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Baghdadian Mu'tazila, when does one deserve reward or punishment?
A) At the moment of the act
B) In the Hereafter
C) At the moment of death
D) At the act with the condition of muwāfāh

Key Takeaway
The Basrian, Baghdadian, ikhtirām, and muwāfāh views represent four different answers to the question of when desert for actions is established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your reward and punishment were decided at this very moment based on your current deeds, would you be content? Or would you need the condition of al-muwāfāh to give you time to repent and rectify?
?
The Baghdadian view delays desert to the Hereafter. Does that make you more hopeful or more fearful about your current state? Why?
?
The condition of al-muwāfāh requires that no act be nullified until death. Are there acts in your life that you have nullified by later sin? What are you doing to ensure your deeds remain intact until your last breath?

Analysis: Praise, Blame, and Deferment
Critical analysis of the views and the argument for deferment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sanusi's analysis of the strongest argument for immediate desert and why full reward is deferred to the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Praise and blame are immediate, so desert is immediate, but full reward is delayed due to incompatibility with hardship.

After presenting the views, al-Sanusi evaluates them. He notes that only the argument linking desert to praise and blame holds some weight. However, he then explains why complete reward must still be deferred. This lesson walks you through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the critical passage
Read the passage from 'وليس لأحدهم تمسك' to 'كما ذكر'. Notice how the author first gives the only support and then provides the obstacle.
2
Understand the analogy
The author says praise/blame are immediate, so reward/punishment should be immediate since they are similar consequences.
This is the strongest argument for immediate desert.
3
Identify the obstacle to full reward
Reward must be pure from hardship, but obligation involves hardship. Combining them in this world is impossible, so full reward is deferred.
4
See the other side's argument
The textualists rely on scriptural evidence and the same incompatibility argument to defer requitals.
The author notes this does not negate desert in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Resolution: Root of Desert vs. Payment
Resolution through distinction and final opinion on partial requitals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sanusi's resolution that the disagreement is about root of desert versus obligation of payment, and the final opinion on what can be given in this world.

Core Idea
The disagreement dissolves when we distinguish between the root of desert and the actual payment.

Al-Sanusi concludes by clarifying that the two groups are not actually contradicting each other. The first group speaks about the root of desert (aṣl al-istiḥqāq), while the second speaks about the obligation to pay (wujūb al-adā'). He then presents a final opinion that partial requitals like honoring believers and legal punishments can be given in this world because they are compatible with obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part
Read from 'والظاهر أن مراد الأولين' to the end of the passage.
2
Understand the key distinction
The first group means the root of desert is immediate; the second means the actual payment is deferred. Both can be true.
3
Examine the final opinion
Full requital cannot coexist with obligation, but partial requitals like honoring believers and punishments can, so they need not be deferred.

Source Text

?

Muʿtazila Scriptural Proof for Eternal Punishment
Muʿtazila scriptural arguments that grave sinners will remain eternally in Hell

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muʿtazila's use of general Quranic threats (ʿumūmāt al-waʿīd) to argue that grave sinners will remain eternally in Hell.

Core Idea
The Muʿtazila cite Quranic verses promising eternity to all who disobey God.

The Muʿtazila school built a strong scriptural case for their doctrine that unrepentant grave sinners face eternal punishment. They pointed to several Quranic verses that use the word 'khālid' (eternal) or describe being repeatedly returned to the Fire. In this lesson, we examine their first proof, which appeals to general threats of eternity (ʿumūmāt al-waʿīd).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: the Muʿtazila present their first proof by quoting four Quranic verses.
The verses are partially quoted; the commentary explains their standard completions.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
List the four verses quoted: 72:23, 4:93, 32:20, 82:14-16. Observe that each verse mentions eternity or being confined to Hell.
Key Arabic words include 'khālid' and 'yusalūnahā' (they will burn).
3
Understand the Muʿtazila reasoning
The Muʿtazila argue that these verses are general (ʿumūm) and apply to all who disobey God, including grave sinners. They take the literal meaning of 'eternity' as indicating permanent punishment.
They do not distinguish between types of sin or the faith of the sinner in these verses.
4
Consider the Ashʿarī response
The Ashʿarīs counter that these verses primarily refer to disbelievers, not believing sinners. They argue that other verses restrict the general threats.
This will be developed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - عمومات الوعيد بالخلود ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم خالداً فيها ( ) ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها ( ) وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار كلما أرادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها ( ) وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين ( )
The Muʿtazila argued with proofs: The first – General threats of eternity: 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell, abiding eternally therein' [72:23]; 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' [4:93]; 'But as for those who defiantly disobeyed, their refuge is the Fire, whenever they wish to come out of it, they will be returned to it' [32:20]; 'And indeed the wicked are in Hellfire. They will burn therein on the Day of Recompense, and they will not be absent from it' [82:14-16].
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the Muʿtazila argument for the eternal punishment of grave sinners who die unrepentant. They cite general verses (ʿumūmāt al-waʿīd) that threaten eternity for those who disobey God, kill believers, or are wicked and defiantly disobedient. The verses quoted are: Quran 72:23, 4:93, 32:20, and 82:14-16. These texts use the word 'khālid' (eternal) or describe being repeatedly returned to the fire without exit. The Muʿtazila take these as evidence that any sinner covered by such threats will never leave Hell. Note that the Ashʿarī response (not provided here) typically distinguishes between disbelievers (the primary referent) and believing sinners, arguing that the threats are general but specified by other verses promising believers eventual Paradise.

Key Terms
احتجت
المعتزلة
عمومات الوعيد
خلود
شديد
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these verses apply to all sinners without exception.
Correction: Ashʿarīs hold that the primary reference of these verses is to disbelievers, not believing sinners, and that other verses specify the exception for believers.

Checkpoint
What is the main type of evidence that the Muʿtazila use to argue for the eternal punishment of grave sinners?
A) Rational arguments about justice.
B) General verses that threaten eternity for disobedience.
C) Consensus of the early Muslims.
D) Reports that the Prophet said all sinners are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Muʿtazila rely on general Quranic threats of eternity to argue that unrepentant grave sinners will never leave Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to take the verses quoted by the Mu’tazila at face value, would you be certain of your own salvation? How does that feeling challenge your reliance on God’s mercy over your own deeds?
?
The Mu’tazila used these verses to argue that even a Muslim who commits grave sins will be eternally punished. Do you ever assume that your own good deeds guarantee Paradise, ignoring the possibility of falling into grave sin? What steps can you take today to protect yourself from such arrogance?
?
Imagine you were standing before God and He held up the verse 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger...' (72:23). Would you feel confident that your disobedience has been forgiven? Why or why not? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of God's justice and mercy?

The Fate of a Sinning Believer: Ash'ari vs Mu'tazila
The author states the Ash'ari view and refutes the Mu'tazila annulment doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on a believer with both good and bad deeds, and the refutation of the Mu'tazila doctrine of annulment (ihbat).

Core Idea
A sinning believer is still destined for Paradise, not eternal Hell.

This lesson addresses a major point of difference between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: the fate of a Muslim who commits sins while also performing good deeds. The author states the Ash'ari view clearly and then refutes the Mu'tazila claim that sins annul all good deeds, using both revealed texts and rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two opposing positions presented.
The passage is the opening of the eleventh inquiry.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the Ash'ari view ('with us in Paradise, even if after the Fire') and the Mu'tazila view ('immortal in the Fire, because evil deeds annul good deeds').
Pay attention to the phrase 'wa-law ba'd al-nar'.
3
Understand the refutation
The author declares the Mu'tazila view 'corrupt' (fāsid) on two grounds: from revelation (sam'an) and from reason ('aqlan). Locate these arguments.
The rational argument uses the example of a sip of wine nullifying a century of worship.
4
Reflect on the stakes
Consider why this debate matters: it affects how we understand God's justice and mercy, and the role of faith versus deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الحادي عشر - المؤمن إذا خلط الحسنات بالسيئات فعندنا في الجنة، ولو بعد النار. وعند المعتزلة مخلد في النار ذهاباً إلى أن السيئات تحبط الحسنات. حتى ذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أن الكبيرة الواحدة تحبط جميع الطاعات. فاسد سمعاً وهو للنصوص الدالة على أن الله لا يضيع أجر المحسنين، وعقلاً للقطع بقبح إبطال ثواب طاعة مائة سنة بشرب جرعة من الخمر ولأن جهة الاستحقاق عندهم ، وهو
He said: The eleventh inquiry - The believer, when he mixes good deeds with evil deeds, is with us in Paradise, even if after the Fire. According to the Mu‘tazila, he is immortal in the Fire, holding that evil deeds annul (tuḥbiṭ) good deeds. Indeed the majority of them held that a single major sin annuls all acts of obedience. This is corrupt from revelation (sam‘an) because of the texts indicating that God does not waste the reward of those who do good, and from reason (‘aqlan) because of the certainty of the repugnance of nullifying the reward of a hundred years of obedience by drinking a sip of wine, and because the basis of merit according to them is...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage introduces the eleventh inquiry of the book, concerning the fate of a believer who commits sins while also having good deeds. The author states the Ash‘ari position: such a believer will ultimately enter Paradise, though he may be punished in Hell first (i.e., wa-lākin ba‘d al-nār). In contrast, the Mu‘tazila hold that such a believer will remain in Hell forever, because according to their doctrine, evil deeds annul (yuḥbiṭūn) good deeds entirely. Some Mu‘tazilites even claim that a single major sin (kabīra) nullifies all acts of obedience (ṭā‘āt). The author declares this view corrupt (fāsid) on two grounds: (1) from revelation, it contradicts texts that state God does not waste the reward of those who do good; (2) from reason, it is intrinsically repugnant that a single sip of wine could cancel out the reward of a hundred years of worship. He then begins a further point about the Mu‘tazilite concept of merit (istiḥqāq), but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
المؤمن
الحسنات
السيئات
المعتزلة
الإحباط
الكبيرة
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a believer who commits major sins will be eternally punished in Hell (the Mu'tazila view), or that good deeds are completely annulled by sins.
Correction: The Ash'ari position, based on revelation and reason, is that such a believer will eventually enter Paradise, even if punished first. Sins do not completely annul good deeds.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on a believer who mixes good and bad deeds?
A) He is eternally in Hell
B) He is in Paradise even if after some punishment in Hell
C) He remains in Purgatory
D) His good deeds are annulled by his sins

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view affirms that a believer with both good and bad deeds is ultimately destined for Paradise, rejecting the Mu'tazila doctrine of complete annulment of good deeds by sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a sin cannot erase years of worship. When you think about your own life, do you truly believe that Allah does not waste your good deeds, even if you stumble? Does this belief give you hope, and how does it affect your daily efforts to do good?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on strict justice: every sin must be punished eternally. But the Ash'ari trust in God's mercy. Which perspective shapes your relationship with Allah? Are you living in fear of justice or in hope of mercy, and how does that show in your choices?
?
Imagine you are the person described: one who mixes good deeds with evil. According to the Mu'tazila, you are doomed. According to the Ash'ari, you are still destined for Paradise. If you really believe the Ash'ari position, what sin are you holding onto as if it doesn't matter? What would it mean to truly repent knowing that God's mercy is greater than your failures?

Ihbat and Muwazana: The Jubba'iyan Doctrines
Taftazani presents the Jubba'iyan doctrines of ihbat and muwazana.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of ihbat and muwazana as held by Abu Ali and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, and the varying interpretations of what is being compared.

Core Idea
Ihbat nullifies the lesser; muwazana balances by removing equivalent parts.

The Mu'tazilite scholars Abu Ali and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i proposed two related but distinct theories on how sins and good deeds interact. This lesson clarifies their positions and the subtle differences that led to further theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
Read the first sentence: both Jubba'is agree that the greater deed (by magnitude) annuls the lesser, regardless of number.
Note that 'annul' here means the lesser deed is rendered ineffective.
2
Distinguish between pure ihbat and muwazana
Find the two definitions: Abu Ali's pure ihbat removes the lesser completely, while Abu Hashim's muwazana removes an equivalent portion from the greater.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the refutation.
3
Examine the three possible objects of comparison
The passage mentions that they differed on whether the comparison is between the acts, between the rewards/punishments, or between the merits (istihqaq). Reflect on why this matters.
This shows the internal debate among Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الجبائيان إلى أن أياً من الطاعات والمعاصي أربت قدراً بحسب الأجر والوزر لا عدداً حبطت الأخرى ، ثم زعم أبو علي أن الأقل يسقط. ولا يسقط من الأكثر شيئاً، وهذا هو الإحباط المحض، وأبو هاشم أنه يسقط، ويسقط من الأكثر ما يقابله. وهذا هو الموازنة ، واختلفوا في أن ذلك يعتبر بين الفعلين أعني الطاعة والمعصية أو المستحقين أعني الثواب والعقاب أو الاستحقاقين واستدلوا على الإحباط في الجملة بمثل قوله تعالى : أن تحبط أعمالكم (١) أولئك حبطت أعمالهم ) (۲) لا تبطلوا صدقاتكم بالمن والأذى) (۳) لكنه لا يثبت ما هو المتنازع من بطلان حسنة كاملة بسيئة سابقة أو لاحقة، فضلاً عن تفضيل الجانبين، واستدل الإمام على بطلان، أما على رأي أبي علي فلأنه تلغو الطاعة السابقة، وهو ظلم عندكم . وينتفى بقوله تعالى : فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره) (٤) مع ما فيه من الترجح بلا مرجح وأما على رأي أبي هاشم فلأن طر أن الحادث مشروط بزوال السابق، فزواله به دور لأنه لا أولوية لبعض أجزاء الكبير، فيلزم أن يفنى بكليته، ولأن زوال كل بالآخر دفعة يوجب وجودهما حال عدمهما لوجود العلة حال حدوث المعلول. وعلى التعاقب يوجب حدوث المعلول بلا علته.
And the two Jubba'is went to the view that whichever of the acts of obedience and acts of disobedience is greater in magnitude according to reward and burden, not in number, it annuls the other. Then Abu Ali claimed that the lesser is removed, and nothing is removed from the greater—this is pure ihbat. And Abu Hashim claimed that it is removed, and from the greater what is equivalent to it is removed—this is muwazana. And they differed whether that is considered between the two acts, meaning obedience and disobedience, or between the two merited things, meaning reward and punishment, or between the two merits. And they adduced evidence for ihbat in general with examples like His saying: 'Lest your deeds become null' (1), 'Those are the ones whose deeds have become null' (2), 'Do not nullify your charities by reproach and injury' (3). But it does not establish the point of contention, which is the nullification of a complete good deed by a preceding or subsequent sin, let alone favoring one side over the other. And the Imam adduced evidence for its invalidity: according to Abu Ali's view, because the preceding act of obedience becomes void, which is injustice according to you (Mu'tazila), and it is negated by His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (4), along with what is in it of preponderance without a preponderator. And according to Abu Hashim's view, because the occurrence of the later (sin) is conditioned by the removal of the earlier (good deed), so its removal by it is a circularity because there is no priority for some parts of the greater over others, so it necessarily ceases entirely; and because the removal of each by the other simultaneously necessitates their existence at the time of their non-existence due to the existence of the cause at the time of the effect's occurrence; and upon succession it necessitates the occurrence of the effect without its cause.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the positions of the two Jubba'is (father and son) on how sins affect good deeds. Abu Ali al-Jubba'i held 'pure ihbat' (complete annulment): if the reward of a sin outweighs that of a good deed, the good deed is entirely nullified, and only the sin remains. Abu Hashim held 'muwazana' (balancing): if one outweighs the other, the lesser is removed and an equivalent is removed from the greater, so the greater remains partially.

They also differed on whether the balancing is between the acts themselves, between the reward and punishment, or between the merits (istihqaq).

They cited Qur'anic verses threatening nullification of deeds, but Taftazani notes these verses do not prove the specific claim that a complete good deed is nullified by a sin, especially if the sin is later.

The Imam (either al-Ghazali or the author al-Taftazani himself) refuted both views. For Abu Ali: nullifying a previous good deed is unjust and contradicts the verse that everyone will see even an atom's weight of good. Also, it would require preponderance without a preponderator. For Abu Hashim: the reasoning is circular because the later sin's existence depends on removing the earlier good deed, which leads to infinite regress or simultaneous annihilation and existence paradoxes.

Key Terms
إحباط
موازنة
أبو علي
أبو هاشم
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ali, what happens to the greater when a sin outweighs a good deed?
A) It is completely annulled
B) It remains entirely
C) An equivalent is removed
D) It becomes half

Key Takeaway
The Jubba'iyan doctrines of ihbat and muwazana both assert that deeds are balanced by magnitude, but differ on whether the greater is partially affected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah were to apply the principle of muwazana to your deeds today, what portion of your good deeds would be removed by your sins? Does that thought drive you to despair or to seek His mercy?
?
The Jubba'is believed that deeds are weighed by magnitude, not number. Do you tend to measure your spirituality by the quantity of your acts (prayers, charity) or by their weight in sincerity and love for Allah?
?
If the ultimate judgment is not a simple tally, but a true knowledge of the weight of each action, how should you prepare for the Day when all will be laid bare? What would you change today?

Refutation of Ihbat and Muwazana
Taftazani refutes the Jubba'iyan views using logical arguments and Qur'anic citations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence the Jubba'is used for ihbat and the reasons why the Ash'ari Imam refuted their views as logically unsound and contrary to the Qur'an.

Core Idea
The refutation shows ihbat and muwazana contradict the Qur'an and lead to logical absurdities.

After presenting the Jubba'iyan doctrines, Taftazani moves to critique them. He first notes that their Qur'anic proof texts do not prove their specific claim, then presents a two-part refutation targeting each version of the doctrine. This lesson analyzes that critique.

Guided Path
1
Assess the Qur'anic evidence
Read the verses cited and note why Taftazani says they do not establish the point of contention.
The verses warn against nullification by one's own actions, not by outweighing.
2
Understand the refutation of Abu Ali's view
The Imam argues that nullifying a previous good deed is unjust and contradicts the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it'.
Also points out preponderance without a preponderator.
3
Understand the refutation of Abu Hashim's view
The reasoning involves circularity and paradoxes: either simultaneous removal leads to impossible coexistence, or successive removal lacks a cause.
This shows muwazana is logically inconsistent.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents the positions of the two Jubba'is (father and son) on how sins affect good deeds. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic verses on nullification directly prove ihbat.
Correction: The verses warn against nullification by one's own actions (e.g., reproach), not by sins outweighing good deeds, and do not address the specific case.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is Abu Hashim's view considered circular?
A) Because the later sin conditions the removal of the earlier good deed, leading to a vicious cycle
B) Because both deeds annul each other
C) Because the deeds are equal
D) Because the Qur'an prohibits it

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of ihbat and muwazana demonstrates that these doctrines contradict the Qur'anic principle of individual responsibility and lead to logical contradictions.

?

How does this truth change the way you view your past good deeds—especially those you may have deemed insignificant or spoiled by later faults? What specific deed will you now take more seriously because of this lesson?
?
If you truly internalized that every good deed remains secure, what would you do differently today—starting now—to increase your balance of good, even in the smallest actions? What is one small, consistent good deed you have been neglecting that you will commit to?
?
The passage points out that nullifying good deeds would be unjust. When you judge yourself, do you tend to focus on your sins and feel your good deeds are cancelled out? How can you shift your heart to trust in Allah's justice that preserves every good deed, and what one step will you take to act on that trust today?

Ihbat and Muwazana: Two Mu'tazilite Theories
Abu Ali and Abu Hashim present two theories on how bad deeds nullify good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite theories of ihbat (nullification) and muwazana (balancing) as formulated by Abu Ali al-Jubbai and his son Abu Hashim, and the key difference between them.

Core Idea
For the Jubbaiyis, good and bad deeds cancel out by weight, not number — but father and son disagreed on how.

This passage introduces a famous internal debate within the Mu'tazila over how acts of obedience and sin interact. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agree on the basic principle that the heavier act nullifies the lighter, but they differ on whether any of the heavier act survives. Their disagreement defines two distinct theories — pure ihbat and muwazana — which have important implications for how reward and punishment are ultimately assessed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the overall argument before diving into details.
The passage is compact but carries a clear structure.
2
Identify the common ground
Both Jubbaiyis agree that the greater act (in weight) nullifies the other. Notice they specify 'by reward and burden, not by number.'
3
Spot the split between father and son
Abu Ali says the lesser is completely nullified, with nothing removed from the greater. Abu Hashim says the lesser nullifies an equal portion from the greater.
This is the core difference: pure ihbat vs. muwazana.
4
Consider the secondary difference
The passage ends with a dispute over what the comparison applies to: the acts themselves, the entitlements (reward/punishment), or the states of entitlement.
This nuance shows further variation within the Mu'tazila.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: how does each view affect the final outcome of reward and punishment? Which one leaves room for some good deeds to survive despite sins?
This connects to broader debates on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الجبائيان إلى أن أياً من الطاعات والمعاصي أربت قدراً بحسب الأجر والوزر لا عدداً حبطت الأخرى ، ثم زعم أبو علي أن الأقل يسقط. ولا يسقط من الأكثر شيئاً، وهذا هو الإحباط المحض، وأبو هاشم أنه يسقط، ويسقط من الأكثر ما يقابله. وهذا هو الموازنة ، واختلفوا في أن ذلك يعتبر بين الفعلين أعني الطاعة والمعصية أو المستحقين أعني الثواب والعقاب أو الاستحقاقين
And the two Jubbaiyis held that whichever of acts of obedience and sins is greater in measure according to reward and burden, not in number, nullifies the other. Then Abu Ali claimed that the lesser is nullified, and nothing is nullified from the greater—this is pure ihbat. And Abu Hashim held that it is nullified, and from the greater what corresponds to it is nullified—this is muwazana. And they differed over whether that is considered between the two acts, i.e., obedience and sin, or between the two entitlements, i.e., reward and punishment, or between the two entitlements.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage explains the two Mu'tazilite theories of ihbat (nullification) and muwazana (balancing) as held by the two Jubbaiyis: Abu Ali al-Jubbai and his son Abu Hashim. They agreed that the greater of the two acts (in terms of reward or punishment weight) nullifies the other. But Abu Ali held that the lesser is completely nullified without affecting the greater—this is pure ihbat. Abu Hashim held that the lesser nullifies an equal portion from the greater—this is muwazana. They also differed on what the comparison is between: the acts themselves (obedience and sin), the entitlements (reward and punishment), or the states of entitlement.

Key Terms

موازنة
جبائيان
أبو علي
أبو هاشم
طاعة
معصية
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both theories are essentially the same.
Correction: Abu Ali's ihbat involves complete annihilation of the lesser without affecting the greater, while Abu Hashim's muwazana involves partial cancellation of the greater.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between Abu Ali's ihbat and Abu Hashim's muwazana?
A) Abu Ali says the lesser is annulled; Abu Hashim says the greater is annulled.
B) Abu Ali says the lesser is annulled completely; Abu Hashim says an equal portion of the greater is annulled.
C) Abu Ali says both are annulled; Abu Hashim says neither is annulled.
D) Abu Ali says only sins annul good deeds; Abu Hashim says only good deeds annul sins.

Key Takeaway
The Jubbaiyis agreed that acts cancel by weight, but diverged on whether any of the heavier act remains — pure ihbat vs. muwazana.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every sin and every good deed has a weight that can cancel the other, how does that reality change the way you view 'small' sins you commit daily? Will you continue to ignore them, or will you actively seek forgiveness and increase your good deeds?
?
The Jubbaiyis disagreed on whether any of your greater act remains after cancellation. In your own spiritual account, do you live with the fear that your major sins have nullified all your good deeds, or with the hope that Allah still preserves some of your good? How does that affect your relationship with Allah right now?
?
When you stand before Allah, your deeds will be measured. Are you more concerned about the quantity of your acts (how many prayers, how many charities) or the quality (their weight in sincerity and adherence to His command)? This lesson reminds us that the weight matters. What specific step will you take today to increase the weight of your good deeds?

Mu'tazila on Lying and the Refutation
Mu'tazila view on specification, abrogation, and lying, then refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that lying only occurs in past tense, and why it is refuted by the definition of lying as informing contrary to reality, supported by a Quranic example.

Core Idea
Lying is defined by correspondence to reality, not by tense.

The Mu'tazila made a distinction between abrogation (naskh) and specification (takhsis), and argued that lying (kadhib) is only possible in past-tense statements. This lesson presents their view and the author's immediate refutation using a clear definition and a Quranic verse that exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the Mu'tazila view on takhsis, naskh, and lying. Notice how they restrict lying to past tense.
The Mu'tazila later scholars consider takhsis with connected evidence as the only valid specification.
2
Identify the definition of lying
Find where the author defines lying: informing about something contrary to what it is. Recognize that this definition applies regardless of tense.
This definition is the core of the refutation.
3
See the Quranic refutation
Read the Quranic verses (Surah al-Hashr 59:11-12) where Allah calls the hypocrites liars for a future promise. Understand how this disproves the Mu'tazila distinction.
The hypocrites promised future support, but Allah declared them liars because their intention was false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بخلاف ذلك الى المعتزلة إلا أن المتأخرين منهم يعدون ذلك نسخاً، ويخصون التخصيص بما يكون دليله متصلاً، ويجوزون الخلف في الوعيد. ويقولون: الكذب يكون في الماضي دون المستقبل. وهذا ظاهر الفساد فإن الإخبار بالشيء على خلاف ما هو كذب سواء كان في الماضي أو في المستقبل. قال الله تعالى: ألم تر إلى الذين نافقوا يقولون لإخوانهم الذين كفروا من أهل الكتاب لئن أخرجتم لنخرجن معكم ولا نطيع فيكم أحداً أبداً وإن قوتلتم لتنصرنكم ثم قال: والله يشهد إنهم لكاذبون لئن أخرجوا لا يخرجون معهم ولئن قوتلوا لا ينصرونهم على أن المذهب عندنا أن إخبار الله تعالى أزلي لا يتعلق بالزمان، ولا يتغير بتغيير المخبر به على ما سبق في بحث الكلام. فإن قيل : فعليه ما ذكرتم يكون حكم العام هو التوقف حتى يظهر دليل الخصوص. قلنا : لا ، بل يجرى على عمومه في حق العمل. بل وفي حق وجوب العموم، دون فرضيته. وهذا البحث مستوفى في أصول الفقه .
The statement contrary to that goes to the Mu'tazila, except that the later ones among them consider that to be abrogation (naskh), and they restrict specification (takhsis) to what its evidence is connected, and they permit breaking of promises (khulf al-wa'id) in warning. And they say: lying (kadhib) occurs in the past, not the future. And this is clearly corrupt, for informing about something contrary to what it is is lying, whether in the past or the future. Allah Most High said: 'Have you not seen those who hypocritically say to their brothers who disbelieve from the People of the Scripture: If you are expelled, we will surely go out with you, and we will never obey anyone against you; and if you are fought, we will surely aid you.' Then He said: 'And Allah bears witness that they are surely liars. If they are expelled, they will not go out with them, and if they are fought, they will not aid them.' Moreover, the doctrine with us is that the informing (ikhbar) of Allah Most High is eternal (azali), not attached to time, and does not change with the change of the thing informed about, as preceded in the discussion of speech (kalam). If it is said: According to what you mentioned, the ruling of the general (al-'amm) would be suspension (tawaqquf) until evidence of specificity appears. We say: No, but it is applied according to its generality in matters of action. Rather, even in the obligation of generality, without it being obligatory (fardiyya). And this discussion is fully covered in Usul al-Fiqh.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the Mu'tazila's position: they differentiate between abrogation (naskh) and specification (takhsis), holding that breaking promises (khulf al-wa'id) is permissible, and that lying (kadhib) only occurs in past-tense statements, not future ones. The author immediately declares this clearly corrupt. He gives a definition: informing about something contrary to its reality is lying regardless of tense. He then cites Qur'anic verses (Surah al-Hashr 59:11-12) where Allah declares the hypocrites liars for a future promise. This refutes the tense-distinction. Next, the author asserts the Ash'ari position: Allah's speech (kalam) is eternal (azali), not temporally conditioned. Therefore, it cannot change based on the time of the thing informed about. He then addresses an objection: if divine speech is eternal and general in meaning, then one would have to suspend judgment on general verses until evidence of specification appears. The author responds: no, the general is applied generally in practice and in obligation, but not as an absolute duty (fardiyya). He concludes by noting this topic is covered fully in Usul al-Fiqh.

Key Terms
تخصيص
نسخ
خلف الوعيد
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lying only occurs in past-tense statements.
Correction: Lying is defined by informing contrary to reality, regardless of tense.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the Mu'tazila distinction between past and future lying false?
A) Because lying is only in the past.
B) Because lying is defined by informing contrary to reality, regardless of tense.
C) Because future statements cannot be true or false.
D) Because the Qur'an only speaks about the past.

Key Takeaway
Lying is defined by lack of correspondence to reality, not by grammatical tense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a promise you made yesterday that you already know you will not keep. According to this lesson, was that promise a lie at the moment you spoke it? How will you rectify that today?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to confine lying to the past to avoid accountability. In what areas of your own speech do you subconsciously try to avoid accountability—by using future tense, vague language, or conditional phrases? Repent now and commit to truthfulness in all tenses.
?
Allah declared the hypocrites liars for a future promise they had no intention of fulfilling. Do you treat your own future statements—contracts, vows, even casual 'I'll pray for you'—as binding commitments? If not, what changes will you make today to align your speech with the reality of your heart?
