Ash'ari Doctrine: Divine Speech is Eternal
Ash'ari assertion of eternal divine speech and resolution of objection about general verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah's informing (ikhbar) is eternal and not time-bound, and how the objection regarding general verses is resolved by applying the general in matters of action.

Core Idea
Divine speech is eternal and does not change with time.

Having refuted the Mu'tazila view on lying, the author now asserts the Ash'ari position: Allah's informing is eternal and not attached to time. He then addresses an objection: if divine speech is eternal and general, does that mean we must suspend judgment on general verses until evidence of specification appears? The answer clarifies the practical application of general rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari position
Read the statement that Allah's informing is eternal (azali) and not time-bound, and does not change with the thing informed about.
This is based on the earlier discussion on the attribute of speech (kalam).
2
Consider the objection
The objection asks: if divine speech is eternal and general, does that imply tawaqquf (suspension) until evidence of specification is found?
This objection comes from someone assuming that eternal speech implies timeless generality.
3
See the response
The author replies that the general is applied generally in matters of action and in obligation of generality, but not as an absolute duty (fardiyya). This means we act on the general until specification is proven.
This response shows that the Ash'ari view does not lead to suspension; rather it preserves practical application.

Mu'tazila Internal Differences on Eternal Punishment
The author discusses the Mu'tazila's famous position that the fasiq is eternally punished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila position on the eternal punishment of the fasiq is not monolithic, with internal disagreements about the conditions for nullification and the permissibility of forgiveness.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are not unanimous on eternal punishment; differences exist.

This concluding passage from the author examines a well-known attribution to the Mu'tazila—that they believe the fasiq is eternally punished. However, upon closer inspection, the author reveals that this is only the view of some; the majority held nuanced positions involving the weighing of sins and rewards, and some even permitted divine forgiveness. Let's explore this important clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read its literal translation. Notice that the text begins with 'خاتمة' (conclusion) and ends abruptly. This is a concluding note on the Mu'tazila position.
The passage is a khatima, so it summarizes a doctrinal point.
2
Identify the popular vs. actual majority views
The author starts by noting that the Mu'tazila are famous for holding that the fasiq is eternally punished. However, he immediately corrects this, saying this is only the position of some. Pay attention to the phrase 'ينبغي أن يكون هذا مذهب البعض'.
This correction is key to understanding the internal diversity.
3
Note the conditions and disagreements
The majority held that major sins only nullify acts of obedience if the punishment outweighs the reward, and this is known only to Allah. They disagreed when they are equal. Also, some groups explicitly permitted forgiveness both rationally and legally, while others only rationally.
The author lists several opinions: Basrans, Baghdadiyya, and al-Ka'bi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة - قد اشتهر من المعتزلة أن الفاسق مخلد، وإن عدم القطع بعقابه إرجاء. لكن ينبغي أن يكون هذا مذهب البعض، إذ المختار عند الأكثرين هو أن الكبائر إنما تسقط الطاعات إذا زاد عقابها على ثوابها . وذلك في علم الله . واضطربوا فيما إذا تساويا ، وصرحوا بجواز العفو عقلاً وشرعاً عند البصرية، وبعض البغدادية ، وعقلاً عند غير الكعبي) من مذهب المعتزلة أن صاحب الكبيرة - بدون التوبة - مخلد في النار وإن
He said: Conclusion - It has become well-known from the Mu'tazila that the immoral person (fasiq) is eternally [in Hell], and not being certain of his punishment is irja' (postponement). But this should be the position of some, for the chosen position among the majority is that major sins (kaba'ir) only nullify acts of obedience if their punishment exceeds their reward. And that is in the knowledge of Allah. And they disagreed concerning when they are equal. And they explicitly stated the permissibility of forgiveness ('afw) rationally and legally according to the Basrans, and some of the Baghdadiyya, and rationally according to others [such as] al-Ka'bi? [The statement:] 'From the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that the perpetrator of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) without repentance is eternally [punished] in the Fire, even if...'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a concluding note (khatima) about the Mu'tazila position on the eternal punishment of the fasiq (major sinner). The author starts by noting a well-known attribution: the Mu'tazila believe the fasiq is eternally punished and that doubting this is considered irja' (a view of postponement). However, he clarifies that this is only the position of some of them. The majority among them held that major sins (kaba'ir) only cancel out acts of obedience if the punishment outweighs the reward, and only God knows that. They disagreed about the case where punishment and reward are equal. Some groups, like the Basran Mu'tazila and some Baghdadi, explicitly permitted divine forgiveness both rationally and legally, while others permitted it only rationally (e.g., al-Ka'bi). The passage ends by restating the known position: the perpetrator of a major sin without repentance is eternally in the Fire. (The text cuts off.) This shows that the Mu'tazila are not monolithic on this issue, though a popular view is that they all hold to eternal punishment.

Key Terms
فاسق
خلود
كبائر
إرجاء
عفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila believe that every major sinner will be eternally punished without exception.
Correction: The majority held that major sins only nullify good deeds if punishment outweighs reward, and they disagreed about equality; some explicitly permitted forgiveness.

Checkpoint
What is the position of the majority of the Mu'tazila regarding the eternal punishment of the fasiq?
A) They unanimously hold that every fasiq is eternally punished.
B) They hold that major sins nullify good deeds only if the punishment outweighs the reward, and they permit forgiveness in some cases.
C) They deny eternal punishment altogether.
D) They postpone judgment (irja').

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are not monolithic on eternal punishment; the majority held that major sins nullify good deeds only if punishment outweighs reward, and some permitted forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you reduced a group's belief to its most extreme formulation without listening to its internal diversity? How can you repent from that habit starting today?
?
If even the Mu'tazila disagreed among themselves on such a weighty matter as eternal punishment, how much more careful must you be when you judge a Muslim's faith?
?
What does it reveal about your heart if you are quick to label a whole sect as beyond God's mercy, while some of them themselves allowed for 'afw? Will you seek to understand before you condemn?

Mu'tazilite View on Grave Sinners and Irja'
Author discusses Mu'tazilite claim that grave sinners are immortalized in Hell, and defines irja'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that grave sinners without repentance are eternally punished, the meaning of irja' as used by different sects, and the balanced stance of tafwīḍ.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites insisted on eternal punishment for grave sinners; the moderate stance is to entrust the matter to Allah.

This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid examines a controversial theological issue: the fate of Muslims who commit major sins without repentance. The author clarifies common misconceptions about Mu'tazilite doctrine and the meaning of irja', distinguishing between extremist positions and the balanced middle path of tafwīḍ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to become familiar with the author's discussion. Note the key terms like 'fāsiq', 'mukhallad', 'irjā'', and 'tafwīḍ'.
Pay attention to the distinction between different Mu'tazilite groups.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where the author explicitly states that the Mu'tazilites hold the grave sinner without repentance to be immortalized in the Fire, regardless of prior obedience.
Contrast this with the earlier clarification that not all Mu'tazilites held this view absolutely.
3
Understand irjā' and Abū Ḥanīfa
See how the Mu'tazilites labeled the stance of not being certain of punishment as irjā', and why Abū Ḥanīfa was called a Murji'.
Reflect on Abū Ḥanīfa's derivation of irjā' from the angels' statement.
4
Distinguish the three positions
Identify the two extremes: the pure Murji'a (no punishment for sinners) and the Wa'īdiyya (certain punishment), and the middle path of tafwīḍ (entrusting to Allah).
This threefold classification mirrors other theological dichotomies like jabr vs. qadr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة - قد اشتهر من المعتزلة أن الفاسق مخلد، وإن عدم القطع بعقابه إرجاء. لكن ينبغي أن يكون هذا مذهب البعض، إذ المختار عند الأكثرين هو أن الكبائر إنما تسقط الطاعات إذا زاد عقابها على ثوابها. وذلك في علم الله. واضطربوا فيما إذا تساويا، وصرحوا بجواز العفو عقلاً وشرعاً عند البصرية، وبعض البغدادية، وعقلاً عند غير الكعبي. من مذهب المعتزلة أن صاحب الكبيرة - بدون التوبة - مخلد في النار وإن عاش على الإيمان والطاعة مائة سنة. ولم يفرقوا بين أن تكون الكبيرة واحدة أو كثيرة واقعة قبل الطاعات أو بعدها أو بينها وجعلوا عدم القطع بالعقاب وتفويض الأمر إلى أن الله تعالى يغفر إن شاء ويعذب إن شاء على ما هو مذهب أهل الحق إرجاء. بمعنى أنه تأخير للأمر وعدم جزم بالعقاب أو الثواب. وبهذا الاعتبار جعل أبو حنيفة من المرجئة. وقد قيل له : من أين أخذت الإرجاء؟ فقال : من الملائكة (عليهم السلام). قالوا لا علم لنا إلا ما علمتنا وإنما المرجئة الخالصة الباطلة هم الذين يحكمون بأن صاحب الكبيرة لا يعذب أصلاً، وإنما العذاب والنار للكفار، وهذا تفريط. كما أن قول الوعيدية إفراط. والتفويض إلى الله تعالى وسط بينهما كالكسب بين الجبر والقدر.
He said: Conclusion - It has become well-known from the Mu'tazilites that the grave sinner is immortalized, and that not being certain of his punishment is irja' (postponement). But this should be the doctrine of some, for the chosen view according to the majority is that major sins only annul acts of obedience if their punishment outweighs their reward. And that is in God's knowledge. And they disagreed in the case when they are equal, and they explicitly permitted pardon rationally and legally according to the Basrans and some Baghdādīs, and rationally according to others besides al-Ka'bī. It is from the doctrine of the Mu'tazilites that the committer of a major sin - without repentance - is immortalized in the Fire, even if he lived in faith and obedience for a hundred years. And they did not differentiate between whether the major sin is one or many, occurring before the acts of obedience or after them or between them. And they considered not being certain of punishment and entrusting the matter to God, the Exalted, that He forgives if He wills and punishes if He wills, as is the doctrine of the people of truth, to be irja'. Meaning it is a postponement of the matter and lack of certainty of punishment or reward. And on this basis, Abu Hanifa was considered among the Murji'a. And it was said to him: From where did you take irja'? He said: From the angels (peace be upon them). They said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' And the pure, false Murji'a are those who judge that the committer of a major sin is not punished at all, and that punishment and the Fire are only for the disbelievers. This is negligence. Just as the saying of the Wa'īdiyya is excess. And entrusting (tafwīḍ) to God, the Exalted, is a middle way between them, like acquisition (kasb) between compulsion (jabr) and (qadar).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the Mu'tazilite position on the grave sinner (fasiq) and the concept of irja'. The author begins by noting the common perception that Mu'tazilites hold the fasiq to be immortalized in Hell, and that withholding judgment on his punishment is irja'. However, he clarifies that this is only the view of some Mu'tazilites. The majority of Mu'tazilites actually held that major sins only cancel good deeds if the punishment outweighs the reward, and this is known only to God. They differed on the case of equality, with some permitting pardon. Then the author restates the Mu'tazilite position more starkly: anyone who commits a major sin without repentance is immortalized in Hell, regardless of their faith and obedience, and they do not differentiate between types of sins. They consider the stance of the Ahl al-Haqq (who entrust the matter to God) as irja', meaning postponement of judgment. The author then explains that it is on this basis that Abu Hanifa was called a Murji', and he recounts the story of Abu Hanifa's derivation of irja' from the angels' statement. Finally, the author distinguishes between two extremes: the pure Murji'a who say sinners are not punished at all (negligence), and the Wa'īdiyya who insist on punishment (excess). The middle path is tafwīḍ (entrusting to God), analogous to the position of kasb between jabr and qadar.

Key Terms
فاسق
مخلد
إرجاء
مرجئة
تفويض
وعيدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that irjā' means denying that grave sinners will be punished at all.
Correction: Irjā' means postponing judgment, not denying punishment. The pure Murji'a who claim no punishment are considered false and extreme.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what basis was Abu Hanifa considered among the Murji'a?
A) He denied that sinners are punished.
B) He considered the lack of certainty in punishment as irja', which he derived from the angels.
C) He rejected divine mercy.
D) He believed that faith alone guarantees Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The balanced theological stance regarding grave sinners is to entrust their fate to Allah, avoiding both the extreme of guaranteed eternal punishment and the extreme of guaranteed forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed without repentance. Do you tend to assume Allah will forgive you anyway, or do you fear eternal punishment? How does the balance of tafwīḍ challenge your assumption?
?
When you encounter Muslims who hold strict views on punishment for sinners, like the Wa'īdiyya, do you feel anger or agreement? Can you see the negligence in the opposite extreme? Where does your own heart lean?
?
The angels said, 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' When you face uncertainty about your own fate, do you honestly trust Allah's wisdom, or do you secretly demand certainty? Will you let go of that demand today?

Mu'tazilite Internal Disagreement on Pardon for Major Sins
Author critiques common Mu'tazilite view and presents internal disagreement on pardon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal Mu'tazilite disagreement on divine pardon for major sins and the author's preferred view that links punishment to the balance of deeds.

Core Idea
Not all Mu'tazilites agree on unconditional punishment; many link it to the balance of good and bad deeds.

The author presents a nuanced picture of Mu'tazilite theology, showing that prominent figures like al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim held a conditional view of punishment based on the weight of good and bad deeds. This passage clarifies that divine pardon may be rationally permissible even for major sins, and the author sets a path for the Mu'tazilites to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Focus on the different positions presented.
Notice the author's critical tone in the opening and closing sentences.
2
Identify the three conditions for judgment
The passage specifies three scenarios based on the balance of reward and punishment: when punishment outweighs reward, when reward outweighs punishment, and when they are equal. Find each condition and the corresponding judgment.
3
Note the scholars and their views
Identify the positions of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, then the exception of al-Ka'bī, and finally the citation of al-Juwayni (Imām al-Ḥaramayn).
Al-Ka'bī is the only one who does not permit rational pardon.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Read the author's final remark: 'we have indeed bestowed this upon the Mu'tazilites so that they may grasp it, and we have set for them a path to follow.' Ask what the author is doing here.
The author is critiquing and correcting the common perception of Mu'tazilite doctrine.

ونحن نقول: ينبغي أن يكون ما اشتهر منهم مذهب بعضهم. والمختار خلافه، لأن مذهب الجبائي وأبي هاشم، وكثير من المحققين - وهو اختيار المتأخرين - أن الكبائر إنما تسقط الطاعات، وتوجب دخول النار إذا زاد عقابها على ثوابها، والعلم بذلك مفوض إلى الله تعالى فمن خلط الحسنات بالسيئات ولم يعلم عليه غلبة الأوزار لم يحكم بدخوله النار. بل إذا زاد الثواب يحكم بأنه لا يدخل النار أصلاً. واضطربوا فيما إذا تساوى الثواب والعقاب وصرحوا بأن هذا بحسب السمع. وأما بحسب العقل فيجوز العفو عن الكبائر كلها إلا عند الكعبي. وذكر إمام الحرمين في الإرشاد أن مذهب البصريين وبعض البغداديين جواز العفو عقلاً وشرعاً. ولقد مننا بهذا على المعتزلة أن ادركوا، ونهجنا لهم منهاجاً أن سلكوا. وإلا فمن لهم بعصمة تنجي أو توبة ترجى.
And we say: It should be that what is well-known from them is the doctrine of some of them. And the chosen view is contrary to it, because the doctrine of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, and many of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqīn) - and it is the choice of the later ones - is that major sins only annul acts of obedience and necessitate entering the Fire if their punishment outweighs their reward, and knowledge of that is entrusted to God, the Exalted. So whoever mixes good deeds with bad deeds and it is not known that the burden of sins outweighs, then one does not judge that he enters the Fire. Rather, if the reward outweighs, one judges that he does not enter the Fire at all. And they disagreed in the case when reward and punishment are equal, and they stated that this is according to revelation (samʿ). But according to reason, pardon for all major sins is permissible, except for al-Ka'bī. And Imām al-Ḥaramayn mentioned in al-Irshād that the doctrine of the Baṣrans and some Baghdādīs is the permissibility of pardon rationally and legally. And we have indeed bestowed this upon the Mu'tazilites so that they may grasp it, and we have set for them a path to follow. Otherwise, for them is there an infallibility that saves or a repentance to be hoped for?
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is the author's evaluation of the Mu'tazilite position on major sins and divine pardon. The author, presumably al-Ṭaftāzānī, states that the well-known Mu'tazilite view is only that of some; the chosen view is different. He presents the doctrine of al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and many later Mu'tazilites: major sins only annul good deeds and lead to Hell if their punishment outweighs the reward; if reward outweighs, the person does not enter Hell at all. This knowledge is entrusted to God. They disagreed on the case of equality, deferring to revelation. Rationally, pardon for all major sins is permissible, except according to al-Ka'bī. The author also cites al-Juwayni (Imām al-Ḥaramayn) that the Baṣrans and some Baghdādīs permit pardon both rationally and legally. The author then remarks that he has clarified this for the Mu'tazilites and set a path for them; otherwise, without such a understanding, they would have no means of salvation (no infallibility saving them nor repentance to hope for).

The Mu'tazili Ease in Repentance and Its Critique
The author criticizes Mu'tazili ease in tawba with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view on the ease of repentance and the Ash'ari critique that it reduces repentance to mere intellectual acknowledgment.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila made repentance easy by removing the need for emotional sorrow.

The Mu'tazila, having excluded major sinners from faith and condemned them to eternal punishment unless they repent, made the act of repentance very easy. This lesson examines their two-tiered view: common believers think mere words suffice, while elites think only intellectual acknowledgment and hypothetical willingness to undo the sin is needed, without any sorrow. The Ash'ari critique highlights the problem of requiring constant repentance without the ability to produce grief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili position on tawba
Read the Arabic passage describing how the Mu'tazila made repentance easy. Note the contrast between common and elite views.
The common view: just saying 'I repent' suffices. The elite view: intellectual acknowledgment and hypothetical resolve, no sorrow.
2
Understand the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'ari critique includes two arguments: (1) The people of Paradise regret without grief, so grief is not required; (2) Requiring constant grief leads to taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of the impossible).
Grief is defined as expecting harm, which is absent when one truly repents.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that genuine repentance in Ash'ari theology requires heartfelt regret, not mere intellectual acknowledgment.
This connects to the broader debate on the nature of faith and sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لما خرجوا بالكبيرة عن الإيمان وجزموا بالدخول، بل الخلود في النيران ما لم يتوبوا، هونوا أمر التوبة حتى اعتقد عوامهم أنه يكفي مجرد قول العاصي : تبت ورجعت وخواصهم أنه يكفي أن يعتقد أنه أساء، وأنه لو أمكنه رد تلك المعصية لردها ولا حاجة إلى الأسف والحزن، لأن أهل الجنة يندمون على تقصيرهم ولا حزن وإنما الحزن لتوقع الضرر ولا ضرر مع الندم . ولأن العاصي مكلف بالتوبة في كل وقت ولا يمكنه تحصيل الغم والحزن، فيلزم تكليف ما لا يطاق.
قال : وهي واجبة .
(وهي واجبة عندنا سمعاً لقوله تعالى : توبوا إلى الله جميعاً. وعند المعتزلة عقلاً لما فيها من دفع الضر، ووجوبها على الفور. فآثام التارك متلاحقة، وقبولها ثابت عندنا بدليل ظني، وواجب عند المعتزلة ذهاباً
And the Mu'tazila, because they excluded the committer of a major sin from faith and judged definitely that they enter, rather remain eternally in the Fire unless they repent, made repentance easy to the extent that their common people believed that merely saying 'I repent and return' suffices, and their elite believed that it suffices to believe that one has done wrong, and that if one could undo that sin he would undo it, and there is no need for sorrow and grief, because the people of Paradise regret their shortcoming without grief, and grief is only due to the expectation of harm, and there is no harm with regret. And because the sinner is obligated to repent at all times and cannot produce distress and grief, this would be an obligation of what is unbearable.
He said: And it is obligatory.
(It is obligatory with us by revelation due to His saying, the Exalted: 'Turn to Allah all of you.' And with the Mu'tazila by reason because it involves repelling harm, and its obligation is immediate. So the sins of the one who delays multiply, and its acceptance is established with us by a probabilistic proof, and with the Mu'tazila it is obligatory definitively.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views on tawba (repentance). The author begins by criticizing the Mu'tazila: because they consider major sins as expelling one from faith and leading to eternal punishment unless repentance occurs, they made repentance easy. Common Mu'tazilis think just saying 'I repent' is enough; their elites think only intellectual acknowledgment and a hypothetical willingness to undo the sin suffice, without any need for sorrow or grief. They argue that the people of Paradise regret their shortcomings without grief, since grief comes from expecting harm, and they are safe. Also, they claim that since one is always commanded to repent, it is impossible to constantly produce distress, making it an unbearable burden (taklif ma la yutaq). The author then states the Ash'ari position: 'He said: And it is obligatory.' The following parenthetical explains: For us (Ash'aris), it is obligatory by revelation (sam'an) based on the Quranic verse 'Turn to Allah all of you.' For the Mu'tazila, it is obligatory by reason (aqlan) because it repels harm. The obligation is immediate. The sins of one who delays multiply. Acceptance of repentance is established for us by a probabilistic proof (dalil zanni), while the Mu'tazila consider it definitively obligatory.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
الكبيرة (al-kabīra)
التوبة (al-tawba)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that repentance requires only intellectual admission and a resolve not to repeat the sin, without any emotional component.
Correction: The Ash'ari critique shows that genuine repentance requires heartfelt regret (nadam) distinct from mere intellectual acknowledgment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Mu'tazili elite view on the requirement for repentance?
A) Saying 'I repent' is enough.
B) Intellectual acknowledgment and hypothetical willingness to undo the sin, without sorrow.
C) Sorrow and grief are mandatory.
D) Only regret is needed, not intellectual acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila made repentance easy by requiring only intellectual acknowledgment, but the Ash'aris counter that true repentance involves heartfelt regret.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila thought acknowledging wrongdoing was enough. Are there sins in your life that you have intellectually acknowledged but never truly grieved over? What would it take for you to feel that sorrow—and are you willing to ask Allah for it?
?
The lesson says sorrow arises from fear of harm. Do you truly fear the harm of your sins? Think of one specific sin you have normalized. Does your lack of sorrow indicate a lack of fear of Allah's punishment? If so, what will you do to revive that fear in your heart?

The Obligation of Repentance: Revelation vs Reason
The author states the obligation of tawba and contrasts its proof and acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili proofs for the obligation of repentance and the implications of immediate obligation.

Core Idea
Ash'aris ground the obligation of tawba in revelation; Mu'tazila ground it in reason.

After criticizing the Mu'tazili ease in repentance, the author states the Ash'ari position: repentance is obligatory. He then contrasts the proof: Ash'aris rely on the Qur'anic command 'Turn to Allah all of you,' while Mu'tazila argue from rational necessity (repelling harm). The lesson also covers the immediate nature of this obligation and the multiplying sins for delay.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement of obligation
The author asserts 'And it is obligatory' (wa hiya wajiba). This is the Ash'ari stance. Read the Arabic and translation.
The pronoun refers to tawba.
2
Compare the two proofs
Note the two sources: for Ash'aris, it is by revelation (sam'an) citing the Quranic verse; for Mu'tazila, it is by reason (aqlan) because it repels harm.
This reflects a fundamental methodological difference in theology.
3
Understand the immediate obligation and acceptance
The obligation is immediate (fawr), and delaying multiplies sins. Acceptance of repentance is probabilistic (zanni) for Ash'aris, but definitive for Mu'tazila.
This affects how one views the certainty of forgiveness.

Three Mu'tazili Views on Reward after Repentance
The author enumerates three Mu'tazili views on whether the reward for obedience returns after repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct Mu'tazili positions on whether repentance restores the reward of obedience, along with their key arguments and analogies.

Core Idea
Repentance does not erase all sin-effects; Mu'tazila debate reward's return.

The Mu'tazila, known for their rational theology, disagreed among themselves on a subtle point: when a person repents from a major sin, does the reward for good deeds done before the sin return? This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid presents three distinct answers, each with a vivid analogy. As you read, notice how each view defines the relationship between the act of obedience and its reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the overall gist of the three views.
Pay attention to the key terms 'ثواب' (reward) and 'طاعة' (obedience).
2
Identify the three positions
Find the names of the scholars or groups associated with each view: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, al-Ka'bi, and the later scholars (some said). Note their core claims.
The first two views are directly contrasted, while the third is a compromise.
3
Analyze the arguments and analogies
For each view, note the reasoning given. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim use a logical argument about non-existence. Al-Ka'bi uses the analogy of sunlight after clouds. The later scholars use the analogy of a tree regrowing after a fire.
Consider why each analogy is chosen—what aspect of the reward-obedience relationship does it highlight?
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Which view seems most consistent with the idea that repentance wipes out sin? Does the third view reconcile the first two? How might each view affect a believer's motivation to do good deeds?
This reflection connects the theological detail to broader spiritual life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت المعتزلة في أنه إذا سقط استحقاق عقاب المعصية بالتوبة، هل يعود استحقاق ثواب الطاعة الذي أبطله تلك المعصية؟ فقال أبو علي وأبو هاشم : لا ، لأن الطاعة تنعدم في الحال. وإنما يبقى استحقاق الثواب، وقد سقط، والساقط لا يعود . وقال الكعبي : نعم لأن الكبيرة لا تزيل الطاعة وإنما تمنع حكمها، وهو المدح والتعظيم، فلا تزيل ثمرتها . فإذا صارت بالتوبة كأن لم تكن، ظهرت ثمرة الطاعة كنور الشمس إذا زال الغيم . وقال بعضهم، وهو اختيار المتأخرين : لا يعود ثوابه السابق، لكن تعود طاعته السالفة مؤثرة في استحقاق ثمراته، وهو المدح والثواب في المستقبل بمنزله شجرة أحرقت النار أغصانها وثمارها ثم انطفأت النار، فإنه يعود أصل الشجرة وعروقها إلى خضرتها وثمرتها .
Then the Mu'tazila differed regarding the case when the entitlement to punishment for a sin is removed by repentance: does the entitlement to the reward of the obedience that that sin had annulled return? Abu Ali and Abu Hashim said: No, because the obedience ceases to exist at that moment. Only the entitlement to reward remains, and it has fallen, and what has fallen does not return. Al-Ka'bi said: Yes, because the major sin does not remove the obedience but only prevents its effect, which is praise and glorification, so it does not remove its fruit. So when it becomes, by repentance, as if it had not been, the fruit of the obedience appears like the light of the sun when the clouds are removed. And some said, and it is the choice of the later scholars: His previous reward does not return, but his former obedience becomes effective in entitling its fruits, which are praise and reward in the future, like a tree whose branches and fruits have been burned by fire, then the fire is extinguished; the root and trunk of the tree return to their greenery and fruit.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argumentation. The author presents three Mu'tazili views on whether repentance restores the reward of obedience that was annulled by the sin. The first view (Abu Ali and Abu Hashim) is that the reward does not return because the obedience no longer exists and the entitlement to reward has fallen. The second view (al-Ka'bi) is that the reward does return because the sin only prevented the effect of obedience, not the obedience itself; once gone, the fruit reappears like sunlight after clouds. The third view (later Mu'tazila) holds that the previous reward does not return, but the obedience itself becomes effective again for future reward and praise, analogous to a tree that regrows after a fire. Thus, the passage shows three distinct positions with their respective arguments.

Key Terms
ثواب
طاعة
كبيرة
توبة
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Mu'tazila agree on the effects of repentance on reward.
Correction: In fact, they have three distinct views: some say reward never returns, some say it fully returns, and some say the obedience becomes effective for future reward but the original reward does not return.

Checkpoint
According to the third Mu'tazili view (choice of the later scholars), what happens to the reward of obedience after repentance?
A) The original reward returns in full
B) The obedience becomes effective for future reward, but the earlier reward does not return
C) The reward is permanently lost, even for new good deeds
D) The reward is transferred to a different act of worship

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila did not have a single view on the effect of repentance on reward; they debated whether reward returns, and if so, in what form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent from a major sin, do you genuinely believe that your past good deeds are still fully credited to you, or do you fear some loss? How does this passage’s range of opinions confront your own assumption about God’s justice?
?
Which of the three analogies—the sun after clouds, the tree regrowing, or the irrecoverable past—best reflects your inner sense of guilt and hope? What does your preference reveal about your understanding of God’s mercy?
?
If you knew that after repenting, some of your past rewards might never return, would that change the urgency or sincerity of your repentance? How does this passage challenge you to rely on God’s grace rather than your own ledger?

Commanding Good is Not Restricted to Rulers
The author states the general rule that commanding good is for all, with exceptions for conflict and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that commanding good and forbidding evil is not limited to rulers, except when it involves armed conflict, and it is a communal obligation.

Core Idea
Commanding good is everyone's duty, not just rulers, unless weapons are drawn.

This lesson tackles a common misconception that only rulers or sinless people can enjoin good and forbid evil. The author argues from historical practice and logical reasoning that it is a general duty, but with an important caveat when violence may erupt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on general rule
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the author states the general rule and mentions the exceptions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ولا يختص بالولاة'.
2
Identify the exception for armed conflict
Find where the author limits the general rule: when it leads to killing or armed conflict, it becomes the ruler's domain.
The phrase 'ربط بالسلطان حذراً عن الفتنة' is key.
3
Understand the communal obligation
Note that the author describes it as a communal obligation (فرض كفاية) and that it can become individual if one is appointed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا يختص بالولاة . (ولا يختص بالولاة إلا إذا انتهى إلى القتل ولا بأهل الاجتهاد إلا إذا كان مدركه الاجتهاد ولا يمن لا يرتكب مثله لأنهما واجبان متميزان ويسقط بقيام البعض عن الباقين لأنه فرض كفاية . وإذا نصب واحد كالمحتسب تعين عليه . وهل يجوز للمجتهد الاعتراض على آخر فى محل الخلاف وفيه خلاف . كان المسلمون في الصدر الأول وبعده يأمرون الولاة بالمعروف وينهونهم عن المنكر من غير نكير من أحد ولا توقيف على إذن، فعلم أنه لا يختص بالولاة، بل يجوز لآحاد الرعية بالقول والفعل. لكن إذا انتهى الأمر إلى نصب القتال، وشهر السلاح ربط بالسلطان حذراً عن الفتنة. كذا ذكر إمام الحرمين وقال : إن الحكم الشرعي إذا استوى في إدراكه الخاص والعام ففيه للعالم وغير العالم الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. وإذا اختص مدركه بالاجتهاد، فليس للعوام فيه أمر ونهي بل الأمر فيه موكول إلى أهل الاجتهاد ثم ليس لمجتهد أن يتعرض بالردع والزجر على مجتهد آخر في موضع الخلاف . إذ كل مجتهد مصيب في الفروع عندنا ومن قال إن المصيب واحد، فهو غير متعين عنده. وذكر في محيط الحنفية (۱) أن للحنفي أن يحتسب على الشافعي في أكل الضبع. ومتروك التسمية عمداً. وللشافعي أن يحتسب على الحنفي في شرب المثلث، والنكاح بلا ،ولي
He said: And it is not specific to rulers. (It is not specific to rulers unless it comes to killing, nor to people of ijtihad unless its means is ijtihad. And [it is not conditional] on the person not committing the like [of the evil], because they are two distinct obligations. And it falls [as an obligation] from the rest when some perform it, because it is a communal obligation. And if one is appointed, like a muhtasib, it becomes personally incumbent. And is it permissible for a mujtahid to object to another in a matter of disagreement? There is disagreement on this. The Muslims in the first generation and after used to command rulers to good and forbid them from evil without anyone disapproving or requiring permission. So it is known that it is not specific to rulers; rather it is permissible for individual subjects, by word and deed. But if the matter comes to armed conflict and brandishing weapons, it is tied to the ruler, to avoid sedition. Thus Imam al-Haramayn mentioned it and said: When a legal ruling is equal in its comprehension by the elite and commoners, then both the scholar and non-scholar have the duty of commanding good and forbidding evil. But if its comprehension is specific to ijtihad, then commoners have no command or prohibition in it; rather the matter is entrusted to the people of ijtihad. Then, it is not for a mujtahid to assail, deter, or coerce another mujtahid in a point of disagreement, since every mujtahid is correct in subsidiary matters according to us; and he who says the correct is one, it is not determined [which one]. And it is mentioned in Muhit al-Hanafiyya that a Hanafi may object to a Shafi'i regarding eating hyena and deliberately omitting the basmala, and a Shafi'i may object to a Hanafi regarding drinking al-muthallath and marriage without a guardian.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses who may perform commanding good and forbidding evil. The author asserts it is not exclusive to rulers (wulat). Historical practice of early Muslims commanding rulers shows this. However, if the action escalates to armed conflict, it becomes the ruler's domain to avoid fitna. Regarding knowledge: if the ruling is equally understood by scholar and layman, both may command. If its understanding requires ijtihad, then only mujtahids may act, and one mujtahid may not forcibly coerce another in matters of ijtihadi disagreement, because according to the author's school (the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on taswib), every mujtahid is correct in furu'. The passage then gives examples from Hanafi jurisprudence: a Hanafi may enjoin a Shafi'i regarding eating hyena (which Shafi'is consider permissible but Hanafis do not) and omitting the basmala intentionally; a Shafi'i may enjoin a Hanafi regarding drinking a certain intoxicating drink (muthallath) and marriage without a guardian.

Key Terms
الولاة
الفرض الكفاية
المحتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only rulers or sinless people can command good.
Correction: The author shows that it is for all, with conditions, and even those who commit sins can enjoin others.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is commanding good restricted to the ruler?
A) Always, because it is a ruler's duty.
B) Only when the situation involves public disputation.
C) Only when it leads to armed conflict and brandishing weapons.
D) Only when the ruler is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
Commanding good is a communal obligation for all, but when it risks armed conflict, it falls under the ruler's authority to prevent fitna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you remained silent about an evil because you felt you had no authority, even though you could have said something? How does this lesson challenge that excuse?
?
The early Muslims commanded rulers without asking permission. Is there a ruler or authority figure in your life today (at work, in community, at home) whom you avoid confronting? What would it take for you to speak truth to them, mindful of the limits this lesson sets?
?
The lesson warns that if commanding good escalates to armed conflict, it becomes the ruler's duty. Have you ever escalated a disagreement to a harmful point? How can you practice commanding good while guarding against fitna in your own relationships?

Knowledge and Authority in Commanding Good
The author discusses the knowledge condition and mujtahid objection, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the level of knowledge determines who may command good, and why mujtahids do not coerce one another in matters of ijtihadi disagreement.

Core Idea
Knowledge determines authority: laymen command what they understand, mujtahids handle ijtihadi matters without coercion.

This lesson explores the nuanced rules regarding who may command good based on their level of knowledge. It also addresses the important principle that mujtahids may disagree without coercion, illustrated by Hanafi-Shafi'i examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on knowledge distinction
Read from 'كذا ذكر إمام الحرمين' to understand when laymen can command and when only mujtahids can.
The key phrase: 'إذا استوى في إدراكه الخاص والعام'.
2
Examine mujtahid-mujtahid interaction
Find the ruling on one mujtahid objecting to another in matters of disagreement, and the rationale of taswib.
Note the phrase 'ليس لمجتهد أن يتعرض'.
3
Review the Hanafi examples
Read the examples about a Hanafi objecting to a Shafi'i and vice versa, and understand how they illustrate the rules.
These examples show mutual objection in permissible acts according to different schools.

The First Mu'tazilite Proof: Actions as Religion
First Mu'tazilite proof and Ash'ari refutation on the transfer of 'iman' to actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that the term 'iman' has been transferred to actions, their proof from the verse 'that is the right religion', and the Ash'ari reply offering alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites used 'religion' to link actions to faith; Ash'aris reinterpreted the verse.

In this passage, we encounter the first of three Mu'tazilite arguments for including actions in the definition of iman. They cite the Qur'anic phrase 'that is the right religion' after a list of actions, arguing that 'religion' here refers to Islam, which they equate with iman. The Ash'ari response challenges this equation and suggests alternative meanings for 'religion' in the verse. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical moves: the Mu'tazilites concede the original meaning but claim a transfer (naql).
The passage is a complete unit: claim, proof, answer.
2
Identify the proof structure
Work through the Mu'tazilite chain: (1) actions are called 'din' in the verse; (2) 'din' is Islam; (3) Islam is iman. Therefore, actions are part of iman.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The reply offers two reinterpretations: the verse could refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or to general religiosity/submission. Also, the equation of Islam and iman is not automatic.
The response does not fully concede the argument; it opens the door for further discussion.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'naql'
Think about what it means for a term to be 'transferred' from its original meaning. The Mu'tazilites argue for a shift in usage; the Ash'aris maintain the original meaning.
This is a key methodological issue in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : نحن لا ننكر إطلاق الإيمان على التصديق بالأمور المخصوصة، لكنا ندعي نقله إلى الأعمال بوجوه : الأول - أنها الدين لقوله تعالى : وذلك الدين القيم ﴾ ( إشارة إلى المذكور من إقامة الصلاة، وغيرها. والدين المعتبر هو الإسلام لقوله تعالى : ( إن الدين عند الله الإسلام (٤) والإسلام هو الإيمان لما سيجيء . وأجيب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك إشارة إلى الإخلاص أو التدين والانقياد ، وأن يراد أن الدين المعتبر عند الله دين الإسلام، وسنتكلم على كون الإسلام هو الإيمان .
And the Mu'tazilites said: We do not deny the application of faith (iman) to assent (tasdiq) in specific matters, but we claim it has been transferred (naql) to actions in several ways: First – that they are the religion (din) due to His saying: 'And that is the right religion', referring to what was mentioned of establishing prayer and others. And the religion that is considered is Islam due to His saying: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam', and Islam is faith (iman) as will come. And it was answered: It is possible that this refers to sincerity (ikhlas) or religiosity and submission, and that what is meant is that the considered religion with Allah is the religion of Islam, and we will discuss that Islam is faith (iman).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three Mu'tazilite proofs that the term iman has been transferred (naql) from its original lexical meaning (tasdiq) to include actions. The Mu'tazilites argue that since the Qur'an says 'that is the right religion' (al-din al-qayyim) after mentioning actions like prayer, and since 'religion' (din) is equivalent to Islam (as in 'the religion with Allah is Islam'), and Islam is equated with iman (to be discussed later), then actions are part of iman. The author (Ash'ari) replies by reinterpreting: the reference in the verse might be to ikhlas (sincerity) or to general religiosity and submission (al-tadayyun wal-inqiyad), not specifically to actions. Moreover, the equation of Islam and iman will be addressed separately, implying it is not straightforward.

Key Terms
إيمان
إسلام
دين
نقل
إخلاص
انقياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the term 'din' in the verse 'that is the right religion' necessarily refers to actions, making them part of iman.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that 'din' can be interpreted as sincerity or submission, not necessarily the specific actions mentioned, so the equation is not forced.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazilites use the verse 'that is the right religion' to argue that actions are part of iman?
A) They claim 'religion' here means iman, so the actions mentioned are part of iman.
B) They argue that 'right religion' refers to tasdiq only.
C) They say the verse is abrogated.
D) They cite it as proof that iman is only in the heart.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof that actions belong to iman by equating 'din' with iman is not conclusive because 'din' can be interpreted differently, and the relation between Islam and iman remains debatable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If 'religion' (din) can mean sincerity and submission in the Qur'an, what does your daily life reveal about the sincerity of your submission to Allah? Do your actions reflect a genuine inner conviction, or are they merely outward habits?
?
The Mu'tazilites were eager to include actions in faith, but the Ash'aris warned against forcing interpretations. Where in your own life are you tempted to reinterpret Allah's words to justify a comfortable belief or practice?
?
If the verse 'And that is the right religion' can be interpreted differently, how does that affect your confidence in understanding the Qur'an? Does uncertainty humble you, or cause you to cling to your own opinions?

Mu'tazili Doctrine of the Intermediate Position and Its Refutation
Author presents Mu'tazili view and refutes it by showing verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view on the fasiq's intermediate status and how the author refutes it by demonstrating that the disagreement reduces to a verbal dispute over the definition of faith.

Core Idea
The intermediate position collapses into a verbal dispute over the definition of faith.

The Mu'tazila proposed that a grave sinner (fasiq) occupies a station between faith and disbelief, not fully a believer nor a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi refutes this by arguing that the alleged distinction hinges on a misunderstanding of the rulings of disbelief and that later Mu'tazila themselves conceded the point. Let's examine the passage to see how the author dismantles this doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazili view is presented. Note the specific rulings they assign to believers and to disbelievers for the fasiq.
Pay attention to the list of rulings for each category.
2
Understand the refutation's core argument
The author argues that the Mu'tazili classification only works if the rulings of disbelief are exclusive to disbelievers. Realize that many of those rulings also apply to some believers.
This is the key logical flaw.
3
Examine the later Mu'tazila concession
The author points out that later Mu'tazila clarified their position: they only deny the fasiq 'perfect faith' (al-iman al-kamil), not faith in the sense of assent or legal rulings. This turns the dispute into a verbal one.
See how this reduces the disagreement.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider the author's comparison to the issue of divine attributes. Understand that the Mu'tazila's retreat shows their doctrine is not as substantive as it initially appears.
The metaphor about 'losing water' is striking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بينهما، وأخذ بما لم يقل به أحد فضلاً عن الاتفاق.
ومنها أن للفاسق بعض أحكام المؤمن المطلق كعصمة الدم والمال والإرث من المسلم ، والمناكحة ، والغسل، والصلاة عليه، والدفن في مقابر المسلمين . وبعض أحكام الكافر كالذم واللعن، وعدم أهلية الإمامة ، والقضاء والشهادة . فيكون له منزلة بين المنزلتين فلا يكون مؤمناً ولا كافراً.
والجواب أن هذا إنما يتم لو كان ما جعلتموه أحكام الكافر خواصه التي لا تتجاوزه إلى المؤمن أصلاً ، كما في أحكام المؤمن، وهذا نفس المتنازع . فإنها عندنا تهم الكافر وبعض المؤمنين وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع، وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم ، وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل، ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وننفيه عن الفساق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة هذا النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر بالاتفاق ، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض، كما يقال في نفي الصفات: أنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الإعراض ، وإلا فقد ماؤهم . يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة، وبأن القول بتعدد القديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره .
Between them, and they adopted what no one said, let alone consensus. Among them is that the fasiq (grave sinner) has some rulings of the absolute believer, such as inviolability of blood, property, inheritance from a Muslim, marriage, washing, prayer over him, and burial in Muslim cemeteries. And some rulings of the disbeliever, such as blame, curse, unfitness for imamate, judiciary, and testimony. So he has a station between the two stations, being neither a believer nor a disbeliever. The answer is that this would only hold if what you have made the rulings of the disbeliever were its specific properties that do not extend to the believer at all, as in the rulings of the believer, and this is the very point of contention. For according to us, they apply to the disbeliever and some believers. In the words of the later Mu'tazila, there is what removes the dispute, because they do not deny describing the fasiq with iman (faith) in the sense of tasdiq (assent) or in the sense of applying rulings, but rather in the sense of deserving utmost praise and glorification, which we call perfect faith (al-iman al-kamil), and we consider deeds in it and deny it to the fasiq. So they have a station between the station of this type of faith and the station of disbelief by agreement. It is as if it is a return from the doctrine and a turning away, as is said in negating attributes: 'We mean what is of the kind of turning away, otherwise they have lost their water.' They declare that one who neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Law, but merely according to language, and that the statement that the eternal is multiple is disbelief without distinction between accident and other.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazili doctrine of 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' (the intermediate position) regarding the fasiq (grave sinner) and then refutes it. The author first states the Mu'tazili view: the fasiq has some rulings of a believer (e.g., protection of life and property, inheritance, burial) and some rulings of a disbeliever (e.g., blame, disqualification from leadership), so he is in an intermediate state—neither believer nor disbeliever. The refutation argues that this distinction only works if the rulings attributed to disbelievers are exclusive to them, but in reality some of those rulings also apply to certain believers (like fasigs). The author then points out that later Mu'tazila have effectively conceded the point by clarifying that they only deny the fasiq the full, perfect faith (iman kamil) that includes deeds and deserves ultimate praise, not the minimal faith of assent or legal treatment. Thus the disagreement becomes verbal: both parties agree on the facts but differ in terminology. The author concludes with a strong critique, comparing this to the issue of divine attributes, and notes that the Mu'tazila explicitly state that according to the Law, one who neglects obedience is not a believer (only in linguistic sense), and that declaring multiple eternal entities is disbelief.

Key Terms
فاسق
منزلة بين المنزلتين
إيمان كامل
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think the Mu'tazili intermediate position represents a real, substantive theological disagreement about the state of the fasiq.
Correction: The author shows that the disagreement is largely verbal: both sides agree on the facts but differ in whether to call the fasiq a 'believer' based on a narrower or broader definition of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the Mu'tazili claim that the fasiq has an intermediate status ultimately reduces to what?
A) A real difference in legal rulings
B) A verbal dispute over the term 'faith'
C) Denial of eternal punishment
D) Affirmation of free will

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili intermediate position collapses when examined closely; the dispute is merely verbal, hinging on whether 'faith' includes deeds or only assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have iman, but do you truly live as someone with perfect faith (iman kamil) that includes deeds and deserves utmost praise? Or are you content with the minimal assent that even a fasiq possesses? What specific deed will you commit to today to elevate your faith?
?
When you sin, do you still consider yourself a believer deserving all the rights of a Muslim, or do you feel you've lost your faith entirely? This lesson shows that even the Mu'tazila only deny perfect faith to the sinner. Are you striving for that perfect faith, or settling for a lower standard?
?
How often do you argue about labels—'Muslim', 'believer'—while ignoring the substance of what true faith requires? If judged by your actions this week, would you be among those deserving 'utmost praise'? If not, what will you change starting now?

Khawarij's First Proof and Its Refutation
Author presents the Khawarij's first proof from verses and responds to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij's proof that every major sin is kufr and how scholars refute it by showing that such terms can be metaphorical or conditional.

Core Idea
Not every sin is disbelief; the term kufr can be hyperbolic or conditional.

The Khawarij were known for their extreme position that major sins constitute disbelief. They supported this with several Quranic verses and hadiths. In this passage, the author presents their first proof and then systematically refutes each scriptural evidence, showing that the apparent meaning is not literal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the Khawarij's argument and the author's response in one go.
Notice the author uses the phrase 'قلنا' to signal his refutation.
2
Identify the Khawarij's scriptural proofs
List the Quranic verses and hadiths they cite. What is their reasoning for each?
They take the literal meaning of 'kufr' in each proof.
3
Analyze the author's refutations
For each proof, see how the author reinterprets the text to remove the implication of literal disbelief.
Key strategies: contextual specificity, hyperbolic language, conditional interpretation.
4
Understand the final principle
The author concludes that 'fisq is not confined to disbelief after faith.' This is the core of the orthodox position.
This principle separates major sin from apostasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخوارج فمذهب جمهورهم إلى أن كل معصية كفر. ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة والكبيرة، وتمسكوا بوجوه :
الأول - النصوص الناطقة بكفر العصاة كقوله تعالى : ( ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون )
وقوله تعالى في تارك الحج : ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين)
وقوله تعالى : ( ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك هم الفاسقون)
الفسق على الكافر، فيكون كل فاسق كافراً
وكقول النبي (عليه السلام): «من ترك الصلاة متعمداً فقد كفر»
وقوله : ومن مات ولم يحج فليمت إن شاء يهودياً وإن شاء نصرانياً .
قلنا : المراد بما أنزل الله هو التوراة بقرينة قوله تعالى: (إنا أنزلنا التوراة فيها هدى ونور يحكم بها النبيون إلى قوله : ( ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله ). فيختص من لم يحكم باليهود، ولأنا لم نتعبد بالحكم بالتوراة . على أنه لو كاد للعموم، فسلب العموم احتمال ظاهر ثم التعبير عن ترك الحج بالكفر استعظام له ، وتغليظ في الوعيد عليه. وكذا الحديث الوارد في هذا المعنى في ترك الصلاة احتمال الاستحلال والمراد بالفاسقين في قوله تعالى : فأولئك هم الفاسقون . الكاملون في الفسق والمتمردون المنهمكون في الكفر، للقطع بأن الفسق لا ينحصر في الكفر بعد الإيمان .
As for the Khawarij, the doctrine of their majority is that every sin is disbelief. Among them are those who distinguish between minor and major sins. They adhere to several arguments: First: The texts that pronounce sinners as disbelievers, such as His saying exalted: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' (Q 5:44), and His saying about the one who abandons Hajj: 'And whoever disbelieves – then indeed, Allah is free from need of the worlds' (Q 3:97), and His saying: 'And whoever disbelieves after that – then it is those who are the defiantly disobedient' (Q 24:55), labeling fisq (sin) as disbelief, so every fasiq is a disbeliever. And like the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever abandons prayer deliberately has disbelieved,' and his saying: 'Whoever dies and has not performed Hajj, let him die as a Jew if he wishes or as a Christian if he wishes.' We say: What is meant by 'what Allah has revealed' is the Torah, indicated by His saying: 'Indeed, We sent down the Torah, in which was guidance and light. The prophets who submitted [to Allah] judged by it' up to His saying: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed' (Q 5:44-45). So it is specific to the Jews in not judging, and because we were not commanded to judge by the Torah. Moreover, if it were general, then stripping it of generality is a possible interpretation. Then the expression of abandoning Hajj as disbelief is for magnification and intensification of the threat. Similarly, the hadith regarding abandonment of prayer carries the possibility of deeming it permissible (istihlal). And what is meant by 'al-fasiqun' in His saying: 'Then it is those who are the defiantly disobedient' are the complete in fisq and the rebellious immersed in disbelief, because it is certain that fisq is not confined to disbelief after faith.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij's first proof for their doctrine that every major sin is kufr (disbelief) and the author's response. The Khawarij cite verses that call those who do not judge by Allah's revelation 'disbelievers,' label abandonment of Hajj as kufr, and call some 'fasiqun,' equating fisq with kufr. They also cite a hadith threatening kufr for deliberate abandonment of prayer. The author responds by interpreting 'what Allah has revealed' as referring to the Torah, specific to the Jews, and argues that calling abandonment of Hajj 'kufr' is hyperbolic and for emphasis. The hadith about prayer is understood as referring to one who deems it permissible (istihlal) to abandon it, which constitutes disbelief. Finally, the verse calling some 'fasiqun' is explained as referring to those complete in fisq and rebellious in kufr, not to every sinner. The author concludes that fisq is not limited to kufr after having faith.

Key Terms
خوارج
فاسق
كفر
استحلال
الفسق لا ينحصر في الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whenever the Quran or Hadith uses the word 'kufr' for a sin, it means the person has literally left Islam.
Correction: The author shows that such usage can be specific to context (e.g., the Torah), hyperbolic for emphasis, or conditional upon deeming the sin permissible.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the verse 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers'?
A) It applies to all sinners universally
B) It is specific to the Jews and the Torah
C) It refers to hypocrites only
D) It is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's proof fails because the verses and hadiths they cite are either specific to certain contexts or use kufr in a metaphorical sense.

Khawarij's Second Proof: Punishment Confined to Disbelievers?
Presents Khawarij's second proof and begins refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij's use of Quranic verses to argue that punishment is confined to disbelievers and the author's early refutation strategies.

Core Idea
Verses restricting punishment need not be absolute; they may refer to its highest degree or be relative.

The Khawarij cite several Quranic verses as a second proof that only disbelievers are punished, aiming to show that fasiqun must be disbelievers.  The author begins his response by offering two interpretive approaches: either the verses refer to the most severe punishment, or the restriction is relative to the righteous.  This lesson examines the proof and these initial counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij's proof
Focus on the three verses they cite and the logical implication: if punishment is confined to disbelievers, then fasiqun (who are punished) must be disbelievers.
Verses: Q 20:48, Q 16:27, Q 92:14-16.
2
Examine the author's first response
The author interprets the verses as referring only to the 'complete and tremendous' punishment. Understand why this is a plausible reading.
The idea: other punished persons receive a lesser punishment.
3
Examine the author's second response
The restriction is not absolute (haqiqi) but relative (bi al-idafa) to the righteous. This means disbelievers are punished in contrast to the righteous, but others may also be punished.
This preserves the possibility of punishment for fasiqun without implying disbelief.
4
Consider the implications
Ask yourself: Which response is stronger? Can both be combined? Note that the passage is cut off, so the full argument continues.
The lesson ends mid-sentence; the author likely goes on to explain further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - الآيات الدالة على انحصار العذاب في الكفار مع قيام الأدلة على أن الفاسقين يعذبون كقوله تعالى : ( إن العذاب على من كذب وتولى ) (1) و إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين ) (2) فأنذرتكم ناراً تلظى لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى (3) قلنا : المراد الكامل الهائل من العذاب والخزي والنار للقطع بتعذيب غير المكذبين، أو الحصر غير حقيقي، بل بالإضافة إلى المتقين ، فلا يمنع دخول

None will burn in it except the most wretched who denies and turns away' (Q 92:14-16). We say: What is meant is the complete and tremendous punishment, disgrace, and fire, because of the certainty that non-deniers are punished, or the restriction is not real but rather relative to the righteous, so it does not prevent the entry of...

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij's second proof: verses that seem to confine punishment (like 'the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' and 'none will burn except the most wretched who denies') suggest that only disbelievers are punished, yet the fasiqun are also punished, so they must be disbelievers. The author's response begins by interpreting these verses as referring to the greatest and most complete punishment, or by stating that the restriction is not absolute (haqiqi) but relative (bi al-idafa) to the righteous, meaning the punishment is confined to disbelievers in comparison to the righteous, but others may still be punished. The response is cut off at 'so it does not prevent the entry of' which likely continues with 'other sinners' or similar.

Misconception: Any verse that restricts punishment to disbelievers means only disbelievers are punished, with no possibility of punishment for others.

Iman and Islam: Distinct According to Hashwiyya
Taftazani presents the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila view that iman and Islam are distinct, with Quranic and hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila argument that iman and Islam are distinct, based on linguistic, Quranic, and hadith evidence.

Core Idea
For the Hashwiyya, iman means affirming reports while Islam means submitting to commands.

This passage introduces a view that sharply distinguishes iman from Islam. The Hashwiyya and some Mu'tazila argue that iman is about believing what Allah's messengers reported, while Islam is about submitting to divine commands. They back this with Quranic verses and the hadith of Jibril. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarise yourself with the Arabic and its English rendering. Notice the key terms: tasdiq (affirmation) and taslim (submission).
2
Identify the core distinction
The Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila differentiate iman as tasdiq (affirmation of reports) and Islam as taslim (submission to commands). This is their fundamental claim.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
They cite verse 49:14, where Bedouins are told they have not believed but submitted, distinguishing the two. Also, verse 33:35 conjoins Muslims and believers, implying they are different categories. Verse 33:22 mentions increase in both iman and submission.
4
Consider the hadith evidence
The hadith of Jibril has the Prophet answering separate questions about iman and Islam, treating them as distinct.
The passage cuts off before completing the hadith quote.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت الحشوية، وبعض المعتزلة إلى تغايرهما نظراً إلى ان لفظ الإيمان ينبيء عن التصديق فيما أخبر الله تعالى على لسان رسله، ولفظ الإسلام عن التسليم والانقياد ومتعلق التصديق يناسب أن يكون هو الإخبار، ومتعلق التسليم الأوامر والنواهي : وتمسكاً بإثبات أحدهما، ونفي الآخر كقوله تعالى : قالت الأعراب آمنا قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا . وبعطف أحدهما على الآخر كما في قوله تعالى : ( إن المسلمين والمسلمات والمؤمنين والمؤمنات .. الآية . وما زادوهم إلا إيماناً وتسليماً والتسليم هو الإسلام . وبأن جبريل لما جاء لتعليم الدين سأل النبي عن كل منهما على حدة، وأجاب النبي لكل بجواب . وذلك أنه قال : أخبرني عن الإيمان،
And the Hashwiyya and some of the Mu'tazila went to their difference (taghayur), considering that the word 'iman' indicates affirmation (tasdiq) regarding what Allah informed on the tongues of His messengers, and the word 'islam' indicates submission (taslim) and compliance (inqiyad). And the object of affirmation is appropriate to be the reports, and the object of submission is the commands and prohibitions. And they adhere by affirming one and negating the other, such as His saying: 'The Bedouins said, "We have believed." Say, "You have not [yet] believed; but say, "We have submitted," ' (49:14). And by conjoining one to the other, such as His saying: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, the believing men and believing women...' (33:35) the verse. And 'And they increased them not except in iman and submission (taslim)' (33:22), and submission is Islam. And because when Jibril came to teach the religion, he asked the Prophet about each of them separately, and the Prophet answered each with an answer. And that is that he said: 'Tell me about iman...'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the opposing view of the Hashwiyya and some Mu'tazila that iman and Islam are distinct. They argue that iman is tasdiq (affirmation) of divine reports, while Islam is taslim (submission) to commands. They support this with Quranic evidence: in 49:14, the Bedouins are told they have not believed but only submitted, showing a difference. In 33:35, both terms are conjoined, implying distinction. In 33:22, believers increase in both iman and submission, and submission is Islam. They also cite the hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet distinguished between iman and Islam when asked separately. The passage is incomplete as it cuts off mid-sentence.

The Mu'tazili Definition of Kufr as a Ruling
Mu'tazili definition and its classification as a ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili definition of kufr and why it describes a ruling (hukm) rather than the essence (dhat) of kufr.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili definition of kufr is about its punishment, not its essence.

The Mu'tazila defined kufr as committing an evil act or omitting an obligatory act that deserves the greatest punishment. The author immediately points out that this actually describes a judgment (hukm) of kufr, not its essential attributes or its self-evident necessary concomitants. This distinction is crucial for understanding the critique that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the definition: 'kufr is commission of an evil act or omission of an obligatory act deserving the greatest punishment.'
2
Note the author's observation
Notice that the author says this definition is 'from the judgments (ahkam) of kufr, not its essential attributes (dhatiyyat) nor its evident necessary concomitants (lawazim bayyina).' Understand the distinction.
Essential attributes define what something is; judgments describe consequences or rulings.
3
Grasp the significance
Reflect on why this distinction matters: a proper definition should capture the essence, not just a ruling or punishment.
This sets up the critique: the definition fails as a real definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : هو ارتكاب قبيح، أو إخلال بواجب يستحق به أعظم العقاب. ولا خفاء في أن هذا من أحكام الكفر، لا ذاتياته، ولا لوازمه البينة التي ينتقل الذهن منها إليه، ومع هذا فإن أريد أعظم العقاب على الإطلاق، لم يصدق إلا على ما هو أشد أنواع الكفر وإن أريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى ما دونه . صدق على كثير من المعاصي، وإن اريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى الفسق (۳) وقد فسروا الفسق بما يستحق به عقوبة دون عقوبة الكفر - فدور . أو بالخروج من طاعة الله بكبيرة ـ ومن الكبائر ما هو كفر - فلا يتناوله التعريف. وإن قيد الكبيرة بغير الكفر، عاد الدور. وبالجملة لا خفاء في اختلال هذا التعريف وخفائه . وما قيل : إن الكفر عند كل طائفة مقابل لما فسروا به الإيمان، ولا يستقيم على القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين أصلاً ، ولا على قول السلف ظاهراً.
And the Mu'tazila said: It (kufr) is the commission of an evil act (qabih) or the omission of an obligatory act that deserves the greatest punishment. And it is not hidden that this is from the judgments (ahkam) of kufr, not its essential attributes (dhatiyyat), nor its evident necessary concomitants (lawazim bayyina) from which the mind moves to it. And despite this, if by 'greatest punishment' is meant absolutely, it only applies to what is the most severe type of kufr; if it is meant the greatest relative to what is below it, it applies to many sins; if it is meant the greatest relative to fisq (and they interpreted fisq as what deserves a punishment less than the punishment of kufr) then there is circularity (dawr). Or if [fisq is interpreted] as leaving the obedience of Allah through a major sin (kabira) — and among major sins is what is kufr — then the definition does not include it. And if the major sin is restricted to other than kufr, the circularity returns. In sum, it is not hidden that this definition is defective and obscure. And what is said: that kufr according to each sect is the opposite of what they interpret iman as, and it is not sound on the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) at all, nor on the doctrine of the Salaf explicitly.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazili definition of kufr as committing a qabih (evil act) or failing to perform a wajib that deserves the greatest punishment. The author first notes that this is a description of the ahkam (judgments) of kufr, not its dhatiyyat (essentials) or necessary concomitants that spontaneously convey the mind to it. Then, an analysis of the phrase 'a'zam al-'iqab' (greatest punishment) shows multiple problems: if interpreted absolutely (mutlaq), it applies only to the most severe type of kufr, not all kufr; if relatively (relative to what is below), it applies to many sins (ma'asi), not only kufr; if relative to fisq (and they define fisq as deserving punishment less than kufr), this leads to circularity (dawr) because the definition of one depends on the other. If fisq is defined as leaving obedience through a kabira (major sin), and among kabair is kufr itself, the definition fails to include those kabair that are kufr. If one restricts kabira to non-kufr, the circularity returns. The author concludes the definition is clearly defective and obscure. Additionally, the Mu'tazili definition does not align with their own doctrine of al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn (the intermediate position), nor with the Salaf's understanding, since kufr is supposed to be the opposite of iman as each sect defines it.

Key Terms
قبيح
أعظم العقاب
أحكام
ذاتيات
لوازم بينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazili definition captures the essence of kufr.
Correction: The author shows it only describes a ruling (punishment) attached to kufr, not its essential attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the Mu'tazili definition of kufr?
A) It correctly defines the essence of kufr.
B) It describes a ruling of kufr, not its essence.
C) It is circular.
D) It is the same as the Salaf's definition.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili definition of kufr focuses on punishment, but it is not a definition of the essence of kufr.

?

When you look at someone who commits a sin, do you immediately label them as a kafir or sinful in a way that judges their ultimate standing? How can you apply the lesson that punishment-based definitions are not the essence of kufr?
?
