Critique of the Greatest Punishment and Circularity
Critique of 'greatest punishment' and circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed critique of the Mu'tazili definition of kufr, especially the ambiguity of 'greatest punishment' and the circularity with fisq.

Core Idea
The phrase 'greatest punishment' is ambiguous and leads to circular definitions with fisq.

The author then examines the key phrase 'a'zam al-'iqab' (greatest punishment) under different interpretations. Each interpretation leads to problems: it either applies only to severe kufr, or to many sins, or leads to circularity with their definition of fisq. The author also notes that the definition fails to account for major sins that are themselves kufr, and overall the definition is defective and inconsistent with their own doctrine of the intermediate position and the Salaf's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of 'greatest punishment'
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the three interpretations of 'a'zam al-'iqab' and the problems with each.
2
Identify the circularity with fisq
Notice that if 'greatest punishment' is defined relative to fisq, and fisq is defined as deserving a lesser punishment, the definitions become circular.
Circularity (dawr) invalidates the definition.
3
Understand the problem with major sins (kabair)
The author points out that some major sins are themselves kufr, so the definition fails to include them. Restricting to non-kufr sins brings back circularity.
4
Consider the final inconsistency
The author adds that the definition does not align with the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn nor with the Salaf's understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : هو ارتكاب قبيح، أو إخلال بواجب يستحق به أعظم العقاب. ولا خفاء في أن هذا من أحكام الكفر، لا ذاتياته، ولا لوازمه البينة التي ينتقل الذهن منها إليه، ومع هذا فإن أريد أعظم العقاب على الإطلاق، لم يصدق إلا على ما هو أشد أنواع الكفر وإن أريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى ما دونه . صدق على كثير من المعاصي، وإن اريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى الفسق (۳) وقد فسروا الفسق بما يستحق به عقوبة دون عقوبة الكفر - فدور . أو بالخروج من طاعة الله بكبيرة ـ ومن الكبائر ما هو كفر - فلا يتناوله التعريف. وإن قيد الكبيرة بغير الكفر، عاد الدور. وبالجملة لا خفاء في اختلال هذا التعريف وخفائه . وما قيل : إن الكفر عند كل طائفة مقابل لما فسروا به الإيمان، ولا يستقيم على القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين أصلاً ، ولا على قول السلف ظاهراً.
And the Mu'tazila said: It (kufr) is the commission of an evil act (qabih) or the omission of an obligatory act that deserves the greatest punishment. And it is not hidden that this is from the judgments (ahkam) of kufr, not its essential attributes (dhatiyyat), nor its evident necessary concomitants (lawazim bayyina) from which the mind moves to it. And despite this, if by 'greatest punishment' is meant absolutely, it only applies to what is the most severe type of kufr; if it is meant the greatest relative to what is below it, it applies to many sins; if it is meant the greatest relative to fisq (and they interpreted fisq as what deserves a punishment less than the punishment of kufr) then there is circularity (dawr). Or if [fisq is interpreted] as leaving the obedience of Allah through a major sin (kabira) — and among major sins is what is kufr — then the definition does not include it. And if the major sin is restricted to other than kufr, the circularity returns. In sum, it is not hidden that this definition is defective and obscure. And what is said: that kufr according to each sect is the opposite of what they interpret iman as, and it is not sound on the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) at all, nor on the doctrine of the Salaf explicitly.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazili definition of kufr as committing a qabih (evil act) or failing to perform a wajib that deserves the greatest punishment. The author first notes that this is a description of the ahkam (judgments) of kufr, not its dhatiyyat (essentials) or necessary concomitants that spontaneously convey the mind to it. Then, an analysis of the phrase 'a'zam al-'iqab' (greatest punishment) shows multiple problems: if interpreted absolutely (mutlaq), it applies only to the most severe type of kufr, not all kufr; if relatively (relative to what is below), it applies to many sins (ma'asi), not only kufr; if relative to fisq (and they define fisq as deserving punishment less than kufr), this leads to circularity (dawr) because the definition of one depends on the other. If fisq is defined as leaving obedience through a kabira (major sin), and among kabair is kufr itself, the definition fails to include those kabair that are kufr. If one restricts kabira to non-kufr, the circularity returns. The author concludes the definition is clearly defective and obscure. Additionally, the Mu'tazili definition does not align with their own doctrine of al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn (the intermediate position), nor with the Salaf's understanding, since kufr is supposed to be the opposite of iman as each sect defines it.

Key Terms
أعظم العقاب
فسق
دور
كبائر
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili definition is clear and straightforward.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the definition is ambiguous, circular, and fails to cover all cases.

Checkpoint
What problem arises if 'greatest punishment' is defined relative to fisq?
A) It applies to all sins.
B) It leads to circularity.
C) It applies only to major sins.
D) It excludes kufr.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili definition of kufr suffers from ambiguity and circularity, making it defective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge someone's faith, do you rely on your own categories and definitions, or on the clear criteria from the Quran and Sunnah? How might the circularity warning in this passage expose the danger of using human-made definitions to label others? Will you commit to asking Allah for guidance before assuming someone's iman or kufr?
?
The Mu'tazila defined kufr based on punishment, but the author shows this leads to contradictions. Have you ever defined 'true faith' or 'disbelief' based on actions alone, ignoring the inner reality? How does this lesson call you to examine your own heart instead of just external deeds? Will you repent from judging others without knowledge?
?
This passage critiques using ambiguous terms to define core beliefs. In your own life, where have you accepted vague or traditional definitions of iman without verifying them from revelation? How can you seek clarity and avoid the error of circular reasoning? Will you take a step today to study the Quranic definitions of iman and kufr?

Imam al-Razi's Argument on Sufficiency of General Faith
Al-Razi argues from prophetic practice and distinguishes principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that general acceptance of Islam is sufficient for faith and that not all theological disagreements warrant takfir.

Core Idea
General affirmation of Islam suffices; details require personal rejection.

Imam al-Razi presents a compelling argument against declaring fellow Muslims disbelievers over theological differences. He points to the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and distinguishes between well-known principles of Islam and disputed ones. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for the concept of takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi structures his argument with a conditional statement and then differentiates between types of principles.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify al-Razi's core argument: if detailed belief were required for soundness of faith, the Prophet would have demanded it. Since he did not, it is not required.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Analyze the distinction
Notice how al-Razi separates principles into those well-known (like resurrection of bodies) and those requiring deep reflection (like the disputed ones). Why does this matter for takfir?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the logical conclusion: if opposing the truth in disputed matters were disbelief, the Prophet would have had to explain them explicitly, but he did not.
Think about how this protects Muslim unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الاستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرائني بكفر من يكفرنا، ومن لا فلا. واختيار الإمام الرازي أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة. وتمسك بأنه لو توقف صحة الإسلام على اعتقاد الحق في تلك الأصول لكان النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ومن بعده يطالبون بها من آمن، ويفتشون عن عقائدهم فيها . وينبهونهم على ما هو الحق منها. واللازم منتف قطعا ثم فرق بينها وبين ما هو من أصول الإسلام بالاتفاق بأن بعضها مما اشتهر كونه من الدين ، واشتمل عليه الكتاب بحيث لا يحتاج إلى البيان . كحشر الأجساد، وبعضها مما ظهرت أدلتها على ما يليق بأصحاب الجمل بحيث يتسارع إليها الإفهام، كحدوث العالم. وإنما طال الكلام فيها لإزالة شكوك الفقهاء المبطلون، بخلاف الأصول الخلافية، فإن الحق فيها خفي يفتقر إلى زيادة نظر وتأمل، والكتاب والسنة قد يشتملان على ما يتخيل معارضاً لحجة أهل الحق . فلو كانت مخالفة الحق فيها كفراً، لا يحتاج إلى البيان البتة . ثم أجاب عن أدلة تكفير الفرق بعضهم بعضاً بأجوبة مبني بعضها على أن خرق الإجماع ليس بكفر، وأن الإجماع لا ينعقد بدون اتفاق المشبهة والمجسمة والروافض وأمثالهم . وبعضها على أن من لزمه الكفر، ولم يقل به فليس بكافر. وبعضها على أن صاحب التأويل - وإن كان ظاهر البطلان - ليس بكافر. ووافقه بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة حذراً عن شفاعة تكفير من تكاد تشهد الأرض والسماء بإسلامهم، وعن لزوم تكفير كثير من كبارهم ، لكن كلامهم يموج بتكفير عظماء أهل الإسلام ، والله عزيز ذو انتقام .ولقائل أن يجيب تمسك الإمام بمنع الملازمة بأن التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إجمالاً كاف في صحة الإيمان، وإنما يحتاج إلى بيان الحق في التفاصيل عند ملاحظتها، وإن كانت مما لا خلاف في تكفير المخالف فيها، كحدوث العالم، فكم من مؤمن لم يعرف معنى الحادث والقديم أصلا، ولم يخطر بباله حديث حشر الأجساد قطعاً. لكن إذا لاحظ ذلك، فلو لم يصدق كان كافراً .
And Master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said: whoever declares us disbelievers is a disbeliever, and whoever does not, then not. And the choice of Imam al-Razi is that he does not declare anyone from Ahl al-Qibla a disbeliever. And he adhered to the argument that if the soundness of Islam were dependent on believing the truth in those principles, then the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him would demand them from those who believed, and would investigate their beliefs in them, and would alert them to what is the truth among them. And the consequent is negated absolutely. Then he distinguished between them and what is among the principles of Islam by consensus: that some of them are such that their being part of the religion is well-known, and the Book contains them such that they do not need explanation—like the gathering of bodies; and some of them are such that their evidences have appeared in a way suitable for the general public such that understanding quickly reaches them—like the temporality of the world. Rather, the discourse about them was prolonged to remove the doubts of the nullifying jurists, unlike the disputed principles, for the truth in them is hidden, requiring increased reflection and contemplation, and the Book and Sunnah may contain what is imagined to contradict the proof of the people of truth. So if opposing the truth in them were disbelief, it would not need explanation at all. Then he answered the proofs of some groups declaring others disbelievers with answers: some of them based on that breaking consensus is not disbelief, and that consensus is not concluded without the agreement of the Anthropomorphists, Corporalists, Rawafid, and their likes. And some of them based on that one to whom disbelief pertains but does not profess it is not a disbeliever. And some of them based on that the one who interprets—even if the interpretation is apparently false—is not a disbeliever. And some later Mu'tazilites agreed with him, cautious of interceding for declaring disbelievers those whose Islam the earth and heavens almost testify, and of necessitating declaring many of their great ones disbelievers, but their speech surges with declaring great ones of Ahl al-Islam disbelievers—and Allah is Mighty, Avenger. And it is possible for someone to answer Imam al-Razi's adherence by negating the implication: that affirming all that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought in general is sufficient for the soundness of faith, and one only needs explanation of the truth in details when one notices them, even if they are among matters about which there is no disagreement in declaring the opponent a disbeliever, like the temporality of the world. For how many a believer does not know the meaning of originated and eternal at all, and never crossed his mind the hadith of the gathering of bodies. But if he noticed that, then if he did not affirm it, he would be a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a discussion on the ruling of takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever). It begins by mentioning the view of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini: whoever declares us disbelievers is a disbeliever, and whoever does not, then he is not. Then it presents the chosen view of Imam al-Razi: he does not declare anyone from Ahl al-Qibla (the people of the qibla, i.e., Muslims) a disbeliever. Al-Razi's proof is an argument from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him): if the validity of one's Islam depended on believing the truth in all theological details, the Prophet and his companions would have required new Muslims to affirm each detail and would have investigated their beliefs. Since they did not do so, it is not required. Al-Razi then distinguishes between undisputed principles of Islam (like the resurrection of bodies) and disputed ones. The former are well-known and do not require detailed explanation; the latter are obscure and require deep reflection. If opposing the truth in the latter were disbelief, then the Prophet would have had to explain them explicitly, but he did not. Then al-Razi addresses the proofs that various groups use to declare each other disbelievers, giving several answers: (1) breaking consensus is not necessarily disbelief, and consensus is not valid without the agreement of all sects, including deviant ones; (2) if a person holds a belief that necessarily entails disbelief but does not explicitly say it, he is not a disbeliever; (3) the one who makes a personal interpretation (ta'wil), even if clearly wrong, is not a disbeliever. Some later Mu'tazilites agreed with al-Razi to avoid declaring many great scholars disbelievers. Finally, a possible response to al-Razi is given: general affirmation of everything the Prophet brought is sufficient for faith, but when one examines a particular detail and rejects it, then he becomes a disbeliever. This preserves the distinction between initial acceptance and later rejection.

Key Terms
تكفير
أهل القبلة
أصول الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any theological error automatically makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that only errors in well-known and clear principles could lead to disbelief, and even then, general acceptance suffices until one explicitly rejects after being informed.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's argument, why did the Prophet not demand detailed belief in every theological principle from new Muslims?
A) Because detailed belief is impossible.
B) Because general acceptance of Islam suffices, and detailed principles are not required for initial faith.
C) Because the Prophet was not concerned with theology.
D) Because only the resurrection of bodies is important.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument shows that general faith is sufficient; only specific rejection of a clear principle after awareness can lead to takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted the faith of someone who disagreed with you on a theological issue? How does al-Razi's argument expose a potential arrogance in your heart, and what would it mean for you to genuinely accept that a person can be a believer despite such differences?
?
When you read the Qur'an and Sunnah, do you assume every issue is equally clear? How does al-Razi's distinction between clear and hidden principles challenge your method of learning and discussing aqeedah with others?
?
Are you willing to extend the benefit of the doubt to a Muslim who holds a view you consider mistaken on a disputed matter? What concrete step can you take this week to build a relationship with such a person rather than distance yourself?

Imam al-Razi's Responses to Takfir Arguments
Al-Razi answers proofs of takfir and a counter-response is provided.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple answers Imam al-Razi gives to arguments for takfir and the counter-response that distinguishes initial general faith from later specific rejection.

Core Idea
Takfir requires explicit rejection; interpretation and consensus nuances matter.

After presenting his main argument, Imam al-Razi addresses the proofs that various groups use to declare each other disbelievers. He provides three distinct answers and also mentions a later response that clarifies when detailed rejection becomes disbelief. This lesson unpacks these nuanced positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the answers
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three answers al-Razi gives, each marked by 'بعضها على أن'.
2
Understand each answer
Examine each answer: (1) breaking consensus is not disbelief; (2) one who is subject to a necessary implication but does not profess is not a disbeliever; (3) one who makes an interpretation, even if clearly wrong, is not a disbeliever.
Consider the basis for each answer.
3
Consider the counter-response
Read the final part where a possible objection to al-Razi is given: general acceptance suffices, but when one notices a detail and rejects it, he becomes a disbeliever.
This refines the argument.
4
Reflect on the overall lesson
Summarize al-Razi's overall position: takfir is not to be taken lightly; it requires clear and explicit rejection of a known truth.
Think about how this protects Muslim unity.

Commentary
This passage presents a discussion on the ruling of takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever). It begins by mentioning the view of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini: whoever declares us disbelievers is a disbeliever, and whoever does not, then he is not. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a person's belief logically leads to disbelief, he is automatically a disbeliever.
Correction: Al-Razi's second answer clarifies that one is not a disbeliever unless he explicitly professes the disbelief itself; implied consequences are not sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the counter-response in the passage, when does a person become a disbeliever regarding a theological detail?
A) Immediately upon the first hearing of the detail.
B) If they have always known the detail and reject it.
C) When they notice the detail and do not affirm it.
D) Only if they deny it publicly.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi emphasizes that takfir requires explicit denial after knowledge, not mere error or implied consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you judged another Muslim’s faith based on a single action or belief. How does al-Razi’s principle—that even a clearly wrong interpretation does not make one a disbeliever—challenge that judgment? What would it mean for you to extend that same grace today?
?
Al-Razi warns that declaring great scholars disbelievers is a grave matter. Is there any scholar or public figure you have mentally dismissed or condemned? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that their Islam is testified by the earth and sky, as the passage says?
?
Consider the counter-response: general faith is enough until you know a specific truth and then deny it. What ‘specific truth’ about Allah or the Prophet have you learned but failed to fully embrace? How will you act on that knowledge today?

Doctrinal Differences and Scholarly Tolerance
Lists three doctrinal differences and condemns fanatics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that verified Ash'ari and Maturidi scholars do not label each other as innovators despite differences in certain foundational issues.

Core Idea
Scholarly disagreement does not imply innovation.

In this passage, al-Sanudi discusses the differences that have emerged between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. He lists three key issues where they diverge: takwin, exception in faith, and the faith of the muqallid. However, he emphasizes that the verified scholars from both sides do not accuse one another of bid'ah or misguidance, contrary to the extremists who consider even minor fiqh differences as innovation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disputed issues
Read the passage and note the three specific issues mentioned: takwin, exception in faith, and faith of the muqallid.
2
Understand the stance of verified scholars
The passage states that verified scholars from both groups do not attribute bid'ah to each other, showing tolerance despite differences.
3
Contrast with fanatics
Fanatics go so far as to consider even secondary fiqh differences as bid'ah. Note the examples given: meat without tasmiyah, ablution from impurity, marriage without guardian, prayer without Fatiha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد دخل الآن فيها بين الطائفتين اختلاف في بعض الأصول، كمسألة التكوين، ومسألة الاستثناء في الإيمان ومسألة إيمان المقلد وغير ذلك والمحققون من الفريقين لا ينسبون أحدهما إلى البدعة والضلالة خلافاً للمبطلين المتعصبين، حتى ربما جعلوا الاختلاف في الفروع أيضاً بدعة وضلالة كالقول بحل متروك التسمية عمداً وعدم نقض الوضوء بالخارج النجس من غير السبيلين وكجواز النكاح بدون الولي، والصلاة بدون الفاتحة ولا يعرفون أن البدعة المذمومة هو المحدث في الدين، من غير أن يكون في عهد الصحابة والتابعين ولا دل عليه الدليل الشرعي. ومن الجهلة من يجعل كل أمر لم يكن في زمن الصحابة بدعة مذمومة، وإن لم يقم دليل على قبحه تمسكاً بقوله (عليه السلام): إياكم ومحدثات الأمور. ولا يعلمون أن المراد بذلك هو أن يجعل في الدين ما ليس منه. عصمنا الله من اتباع الهوى وثبتنا على اقتفاء الهدى بالنبي وآله.
And now there has arisen between the two groups disagreement in some foundational matters, such as the issue of takwin (the act of bringing into existence), the issue of exception in faith, the issue of the faith of the muqallid (the one who follows blindly), and other than that. But the verified scholars from the two groups do not attribute one another to innovation (bid'a) and misguidance, contrary to the invalidators and fanatics, who perhaps even consider differences in secondary matters (furu') as innovation and misguidance, such as the opinion on the permissibility of eating meat on which the name of Allah was intentionally not mentioned, and the non-invalidation of ablution due to impure discharge other than from the two passages, and the permissibility of marriage without a guardian, and prayer without al-Fatiha. And they do not know that blameworthy innovation is that which is newly introduced into religion, without having been in the time of the Companions and Followers, and for which no legal evidence indicates its prohibition. And among the ignorant are those who consider every matter that did not exist in the time of the Companions as blameworthy innovation, even if no evidence for its reprehensibility has been established, clinging to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Beware of newly invented matters.' And they do not know that what is meant by that is to introduce into religion what is not part of it. May Allah protect us from following caprice and establish us upon following guidance through the Prophet and his family.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage addresses the differences between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. It lists three major doctrinal issues: takwin (whether the attribute of creation is eternal or temporal), exception in faith (whether one can say 'I am a believer if Allah wills'), and faith of the muqallid (whether one who accepts faith without proofs is a believer). It then states that verified scholars from both sides do not accuse each other of bid'ah, unlike fanatics who even elevate minor fiqh disagreements to the level of bid'ah. It then defines blameworthy innovation: something introduced into religion that did not exist in early times and has no evidence. It criticizes those who reject all new things simply because they are new, misunderstanding the hadith about invented matters. The passage concludes with a supplication.

Key Terms
التكوين
الاستثناء في الإيمان
إيمان المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All differences between Ash'aris and Maturidis make one group deviant.
Correction: Verified scholars do not accuse each other of bid'ah despite disagreements.

Checkpoint
What do the verified scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) from the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools do regarding their differences?
A) They accuse each other of bid'ah.
B) They do not attribute one another to innovation and misguidance.
C) They only differ in foundational matters.
D) They consider all differences as bid'ah.

Key Takeaway
Verified Ash'ari and Maturidi scholars maintain mutual respect and do not declare innovation despite doctrinal differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you were quick to label someone's action as bid'ah. What was the basis of your judgment? Would the verified scholars in this passage approve of your attitude? How can you change your approach today to be more in line with the sunnah of scholarly respect?

The True Meaning of Blameworthy Bid'ah
Defines blameworthy bid'ah and refutes extremist views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that blameworthy bid'ah is only what is introduced into religion without evidence, not merely anything new.

Core Idea
Bid'ah is about adding to religion, not about new practices per se.

After condemning extremists who label everything new as bid'ah, al-Sanudi provides the correct definition: blameworthy innovation is something newly introduced into religion that did not exist in the time of the Companions and for which no legal evidence indicates its prohibition. He then refutes those who misunderstand the prophetic hadith 'Beware of invented matters' and apply it to all novelties.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of blameworthy bid'ah
The author defines it as something newly introduced into religion, not present in early times, and for which no evidence indicates prohibition.
2
Identify the extreme view
Some ignorant people consider everything that did not exist in the time of the Companions as bid'ah, even if no evidence exists for its reprehensibility.
3
Understand the correct interpretation of the hadith
The hadith 'Beware of invented matters' refers to adding to religion what is not from it, not to every new worldly practice.

The Correct Definition of Blameworthy Innovation
Refutes extremists and defines blameworthy innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of blameworthy innovation (bid'a madhmumah) and how it differs from the extremist view that labels all new practices as bid'a.

Core Idea
Blameworthy innovation is inserting into religion what is not from it, not every new practice.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a common extremist error: equating all differences of opinion or new practices with blameworthy innovation. The author clarifies the correct definition of bid'a madhmumah and distinguishes between legitimate differences and actual innovation. He also refutes the misuse of the hadith 'Beware of newly invented matters.'

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by mentioning two groups (Maturidis and Ash'aris) that have differences in some principles like takwin and istithna', but the investigators do not label each other as innovators. Note that bigoted extremists go further by calling subsidiary differences bid'a.
This sets up the problem of extremism.
2
Identify the correct definition
The author defines blameworthy innovation as what is newly introduced in religion without being from the era of the Companions and without legal proof. Memorize this definition as the standard.
The definition includes both temporal and legal criteria.
3
Reflect on the misinterpretation of the hadith
Extremists misuse the hadith 'Beware of newly invented matters' to condemn all new things. The author clarifies that the hadith means inserting into religion what is not from it.
This prevents misapplication of the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سليمان الجوزجاني تلميذ محمد بن الحسن الشيباني رحمه الله وماتريد من قری سمرقند، وقد دخل الآن فيها بين الطائفتين اختلاف في بعض الأصول، كمسألة التكوين، ومسألة الاستثناء في الإيمان ومسألة إيمان المقلد وغير ذلك والمحققون من الفريقين لا ينسبون أحدهما إلى البدعة والضلالة خلافاً للمبطلين المتعصبين، حتى ربما جعلوا الاختلاف في الفروع أيضاً بدعة وضلالة كالقول بحل متروك التسمية عمداً وعدم نقض الوضوء بالخارج النجس من غير السبيلين وكجواز النكاح بدون الولي، والصلاة بدون الفاتحة ولا يعرفون أن البدعة المذمومة هو المحدث في الدين، من غير أن يكون في عهد الصحابة والتابعين ولا دل عليه الدليل الشرعي. ومن الجهلة من يجعل كل أمر لم يكن في زمن الصحابة بدعة مذمومة، وإن لم يقم دليل على قبحه تمسكاً بقوله عليه السلام: إياكم ومحدثات الأمور. ولا يعلمون أن المراد بذلك هو أن يجعل في الدين ما ليس منه. عصمنا الله من اتباع الهوى وثبتنا على اقتفاء الهدى بالنبي وآله.
Sulayman al-Juzjani, the student of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani (may Allah have mercy on him), and Maturid from the villages of Samarkand. Now there has entered between the two groups disagreement in some principles, such as the issue of takwin (origination), the issue of istithna' in faith, the issue of the faith of the blind follower, and other than that. And the investigators from the two groups do not attribute one of them to innovation (bid'a) and misguidance, contrary to the false claimants (mubtilun) who are bigoted, so much so that they perhaps make disagreement in subsidiary issues also innovation and misguidance, like the saying that meat over which the name of Allah has been intentionally omitted is permissible, and that ablution is not nullified by impure substance exiting from other than the two passages, and the permissibility of marriage without a guardian, and prayer without al-Fatihah. And they do not know that blameworthy innovation is what is newly introduced in religion, without it being in the era of the Companions and Successors, and no legal proof indicates its praiseworthiness. And among the ignorant are those who make every matter that was not present in the time of the Companions a blameworthy innovation, even if no proof for its ugliness stands, clinging to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Beware of newly invented matters.' And they do not know that what is meant by that is to put into religion that which is not from it. May Allah protect us from following desires and establish us upon following guidance, through the Prophet and his family.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage serves as a refutation of extremists who equate all differences of opinion with innovation and misguidance. It begins by mentioning that the two groups (likely Maturidis and Ash'aris) have differences in some principles such as takwin, istithna', and the faith of the muqallid, but the investigators do not accuse each other of bid'a. However, the bigoted false claimants go so far as to label subsidiary differences as innovation, citing examples like the permissibility of meat without basmalah, etc. Then the author defines blameworthy innovation correctly: it is what is newly introduced in religion without being present in the era of the Companions and without legal proof. He criticizes those who make every new thing a bid'a, misinterpreting the hadith 'Beware of newly invented matters,' which actually refers to inserting into religion what is not from it. The passage ends with a prayer for protection from following desires.

Key Terms

محدثات الأمور
مقلد
التكوين
الاستثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every new practice or difference of opinion is blameworthy innovation (bid'a madhmumah).
Correction: Blameworthy innovation is only what is newly introduced in religion without being from the time of the Companions and without legal proof. Legitimate differences and new practices that have a basis are not bid'a.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct definition of blameworthy innovation (bid'a madhmumah)?
A) Anything not done by the Prophet ﷺ.
B) Something newly introduced in religion without being from the era of the Companions and without legal proof.
C) Any disagreement among scholars.
D) Any practice that contradicts one's own school.

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of blameworthy innovation (bid'a madhmumah) is that which is newly introduced in religion without being from the era of the Companions and without any legal proof indicating its praiseworthiness.

Is there an area of your own understanding of Islam where you might be clinging to a surface-level interpretation without looking deeper, potentially causing you to wrong others? Will you commit to re-examining one such issue this week?

Definition of Imamate and Its Inclusion in Kalam
The author defines imamate and explains its inclusion in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason why the science of kalam includes the topic of imamate and the precise definition of imamate with its key conditions.

Core Idea
Imamate is discussed in kalam because corrupt beliefs about it threatened the foundations of Islam.

In this passage, the author explains that the inclusion of imamate in kalam was not arbitrary. Corrupt beliefs, especially from sects like the Rawāfiḍ and Khawārij, had spread to the point of undermining Islamic principles. Therefore, the mutakallimūn added imamate to the subjects of kalam and defined it precisely as a general leadership as caliphate from the Prophet, excluding prophethood, local leadership, and deputy leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. The passage first gives background on why imamate is in kalam, then defines it. Notice the key terms.
2
Understand the historical context
The author mentions that corrupt beliefs from the Rawāfiḍ and Khawārij spread, causing sectarianism. This is why imamate became a theological topic.
Sectarian disputes about imamate threatened Islamic unity.
3
Analyze the definition of imamate
The definition includes three conditions: 'general leadership', 'in religion and this world', and 'as caliphate from the Prophet'. Understand how each condition excludes other types of leadership.
Excluded: prophethood, judgeship/local rule, and deputy leadership.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why defining imamate precisely was important for Islamic orthodoxy. This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن سوء صنيعه بالقول، فلأهل الحل والعقد التواطؤ على ردعه ولو بشهر السلاح ونصب الحروب هذا ، ولكن لما شاعت بين الناس في باب الإمامة اعتقادات فاسدة واختلافات بل اختلافات باردة سيما من فرق الروافض والخوارج ومالت كل فئة إلى تعصبات تكاد تفضي إلى رفض كثير من قواعد الإسلام، ونقض عقائد المسلمين والقدح في الخلفاء الراشدين، مع القطع بأنه ليس للبحث عن أحوالهم، واستحقاقهم وأفضليتهم كثير تعلق بأفعال المكلفين ألحق المتكلمون هذا الباب بأبواب الكلام ، وربما أدرجوه في تعريفه حيث قالوا : هو العلم الباحث عن أصول الصانع والنبوه والإمامة والمعاد، وما يتصل بذلك على قانون الإسلام والإمامة رئاسة عامة في أمر الدين والدنيا ، خلافة عن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وبهذا القيد خرجت النبوة، وبقيد العموم مثل القضاء والرياسة في بعض النواحي، وكذا رئاسة من جعلة الإمام نائباً عنه على الإطلاق، فإنها لا تعم الإمامة.
From the evil of his deed by speech, the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd) have the agreement to restrain him, even by brandishing weapons and waging wars. But when corrupt beliefs and differences, indeed cold differences, spread among people concerning the chapter of imamate, especially from the sects of the Rawāfiḍ and Khawārij, and each faction inclined to fanaticisms that almost lead to rejecting many of the foundations of Islam, undermining the beliefs of Muslims, and defaming the Rightly Guided Caliphs, while being certain that investigating their states, merit, and superiority does not have much connection to the actions of the accountable, the mutakallimūn attached this chapter to the chapters of kalām, and perhaps they included it in its definition where they said: It is the science that investigates the principles of the Creator, prophethood, imamate, and the return (al-maʿād), and what pertains to that according to the law of Islam. And imamate is a general leadership in matters of religion and this world, as a caliphate from the Prophet (peace be upon him). By this restriction, prophethood is excluded, and by the restriction of generality, positions like judgeship and leadership in some regions are excluded, as well as the leadership of one whom the Imam has made a deputy absolutely, for it is not general imamate.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage explains the reason for including the topic of imamate in kalām and provides its definition. The author begins by describing how, despite the ability of the community's leaders to restrain wrongdoers, harmful beliefs about imamate spread among the people, especially from the Rawāfiḍ and Khawārij, leading to sectarianism that threatened Islamic foundations. Because these issues had serious consequences, the mutakallimūn added the discussion of imamate to the subjects of kalām, and some even included it in the very definition of kalām, which encompasses the principles of the Creator, prophethood, imamate, and the afterlife. The author then defines imamate as a general leadership in religious and worldly affairs, as a caliphate from the Prophet. He clarifies the definition's conditions: the phrase 'as a caliphate' excludes prophethood (since prophets are not caliphs), the word 'general' excludes specific offices like judgeship or regional leadership, and the condition of generality also excludes the leadership of a deputy appointed by the Imam, which is not universal imamate.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الرئاسة العامة
الخلافة
أهل الحل والعقد
الكلام
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imamate is only a political matter and not relevant to theology.
Correction: The author shows that imamate is a theological topic because corrupt beliefs about it threatened the foundations of Islam, thus it is included in kalām.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was the topic of imamate included in the science of kalām?
A) Because it is a purely political matter
B) Because corrupt beliefs about imamate spread, threatening Islamic foundations
C) Because it is part of the definition of prophethood
D) Because it has no connection to the actions of the accountable

Key Takeaway
Imamate is discussed in kalam because of its theological importance, and it is defined as general leadership as caliphate from the Prophet, excluding prophethood, local offices, and deputy leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the 'corrupt beliefs' and 'fanaticisms' that led to rejecting Islamic foundations, do you feel that your own attachments to a particular group or leader blind you to the broader truths of Islam? What concrete step can you take today to guard against such partisanship?
?
The author emphasizes that imamate is a general leadership in religion and worldly affairs as a caliphate from the Prophet. Does your understanding of leadership in the Muslim community give proper weight to both religion and worldly matters, or do you tend to emphasize one at the expense of the other? How will you adjust?
?
The passage says that investigating the states of the caliphs does not have much connection to the actions of the accountable. Do you spend time debating historical figures of the past while neglecting your own obligations to Allah? What is one action you will take today that is more important than such debates?

Al-Razi's Definition of Imamate
Al-Razi's definition of imamate with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's definition of imamate, the objection regarding unity as a condition versus an essential constituent, and the response to the caliphate objection.

Core Idea
Imamate is general leadership for one person; unity is a strong condition, not an essential part.

Al-Razi offers a precise definition of imamate as general leadership in religion and worldly affairs for a single person. The passage then examines an objection that unity is merely a condition, not an essential constituent, and clarifies why it functions like an essential one. Finally, it addresses a concern about the caliphate being limited to direct prophetic appointment.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Razi's definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Al-Razi defines imamate as 'general leadership' (riyāsa ʿāmma) and specifies 'for a person among persons' to exclude collective leadership.
Pay attention to the phrase 'احتراز عن كل الأمة' – it distinguishes imamate from leadership of the whole community.
2
Understand the objection and response about unity
Identify the objection that unity (al-waḥda) is a condition (shart) not an essential constituent (muqawwim). Then see the author's counter: because the entire community would not be called 'imams', unity resembles an essential constituent.
The distinction between 'شرائط' (conditions) and 'مقومات' (essentials) is key in Islamic legal theory.
3
Examine the caliphate objection and answer
Read the second objection: that caliphate only applies to those directly appointed by the Prophet. Then the answer: 'caliphate' is more general and can be through an intermediary or directly.
The term 'خلافة' is broader than just direct succession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الرازي (۱) : هي رياسة عامة في الدين والدنيا، لشخص من الأشخاص، وقال : هو احتراز عن كل الأمة إذا عزلوا الإمام لفسقه . وكأنه أراد بكل الأمة أهل الحل والعقد، واعتبر رياستهم على من عداهم أو على كل من آحاد الأمة. ومع هذا يرد ، عليه أن الوحدة من شرائط الإمامة لا من مقوماتها. وفي الشروط كثرة وعلى اشتراطها أدلة ويمكن أن يقال: إنها بالمقومات أشبه من جهة أنه لا يقال لجميع الأمة حينئذ أئمة . بخلاف الإمام الجاهل أو الفاسق أو نحو ذلك، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن لا يقال لشخصين بايعهما الأمة أنهما إمامان . فإن قيل : الخلافة عن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إنما تكون فيمن استخلفه النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) . ولا يصدق التعريف على إمامة البيعة (۲) ونحوها ، فضلاً عن رئاسة النائب العام للإمام . قلنا : لو سلم فالاستخلاف أعم من أن يكون بوسط أو بدونه .
And Imam al-Rāzī (may God have mercy on him) said: It is a general leadership in religion and this world, for a person among persons. And he said: It is a precaution against the entire community if they depose the Imam due to his corruption. It seems he meant by 'the entire community' the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd), and he considered their leadership over others or over every individual of the community. Nevertheless, it can be objected that unity (al-waḥda) is among the conditions (shurūṭ) of imamate, not among its essential constituents (muqawwimāt). There are many conditions, and there are proofs for requiring them. And it can be said that it is more like essential constituents in that the entire community would not be called 'imams' at that time, unlike an ignorant or corrupt Imam or the like. And on this basis, two individuals whom the community has pledged allegiance should not be called 'two imams.' If it is said: The caliphate from the Prophet (peace be upon him) is only for one whom the Prophet appointed as caliph, and the definition does not apply to the imamate of the pledge of allegiance (al-bayʿa) and the like, let alone the leadership of the general deputy of the Imam. We say: Even if granted, the caliphate is more general, whether it be with an intermediary or without.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents and discusses al-Rāzī's definition of imamate. Al-Rāzī defines imamate as a general leadership in religion and this world for a single person. He adds that this definition excludes the case where the entire community (i.e., its representatives) deposes an Imam due to his corruption, because then the leadership would be with them, not a single Imam. The author then raises an objection: unity (having one leader) is a condition of imamate, not part of its essential definition. However, he notes that this condition is so important that it resembles an essential constituent, as we would not call an entire community 'imams', unlike an ignorant or corrupt Imam who still could be called an Imam. Therefore, two individuals who both receive allegiance should not be called Imams. Next, an objection is posed: the caliphate is specifically for those directly appointed by the Prophet, and the definition would not cover imamate established through bayʿa (allegiance) or the general deputy. The answer is that the term 'caliphate' (khilāfa) is general and can be through an intermediary (like a caliph's appointment) or directly.

Key Terms

الوحدة

أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity (single leader) is an essential part of the definition of imamate.
Correction: The author clarifies that unity is a condition (shart) of imamate, not an essential constituent (muqawwim), but it resembles an essential because we would not call the entire community 'imams'.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rāzī's definition, what does the condition of 'a single person' exclude?
A) The Prophet's caliphate
B) Leadership of the entire community if they depose the Imam
C) The general deputy of the Imam
D) A corrupt Imam

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines imamate as general leadership for one person; the passage clarifies the role of unity as a condition and defends the definition against objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage highlights that unity in leadership is so crucial it nearly becomes part of the essence. Reflect on your own spiritual life: are you truly united under one authority—the Quran and Sunnah as interpreted by the rightly guided—or do you allow multiple conflicting sources to fragment your loyalty? What specific step can you take today to strengthen that unity?
?
The objection that caliphate requires direct prophetic appointment suggests a desire for immediate, tangible connection to the Prophet. Do you feel that distance from the prophetic era diminishes the authority of our current scholars and leaders? How can you cultivate a sense of direct responsibility to the Prophet's mission, even though you live centuries later?
?
Al-Razi's definition emphasizes leadership in 'religion and this world.' Is your faith integrated into every aspect of your life, or do you compartmentalize it? Which area of your worldly affairs (work, family, finances) most resists being under the general leadership of Islamic principles, and what is one concrete change you can make to bring it into submission?
